Inheritance of Four Daughters and Two Wives: Applying Radd
Inheritance puzzle illustrating radd with four daughters and two wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate inheritance shares for a case of four daughters and two wives and apply the principle of radd when no asabah exists.

Core Idea
When only Quranic heirs exist and no asabah, the surplus is returned proportionally to the Quranic heirs (except spouses).

This lesson presents a classical inheritance puzzle illustrating the principle of radd. The deceased leaves four daughters and two wives. After assigning fixed Quranic shares (two-thirds for daughters and one-eighth for wives), a surplus remains. Because there is no residuary heir (asabah), the surplus is returned to the daughters proportionally. This case shows how radd operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify all surviving heirs
List the heirs: four daughters and two wives. Note the family relationships: the deceased married two women (the grandmothers of the questioner) and each wife had two daughters.
The daughters are the only children; wives are not blood relatives of each other.
2
Assign the fixed Quranic shares
Two or more daughters receive 2/3 (fard). Two or more wives receive 1/8 (fard) when there are children.
These shares are fixed by Quran (4:11, 4:12).
3
Find a common denominator and compute shares in fractions
Use denominator 24: daughters 2/3 = 16/24, wives 1/8 = 3/24. Sum is 19/24.
The LCM of 3 and 8 is 24.
4
Determine surplus or deficit
Total shares (19/24) are less than the estate (24/24), leaving a surplus of 5/24.
If the sum exceeded 1, we would apply 'awl' (proportional reduction). Here we apply radd.
5
Apply radd to distribute surplus
Since no asabah exists, the surplus (5/24) is returned to the daughters in proportion to their share. Daughters get 16/24 + 5/24 = 21/24. Wives keep 3/24.
Spouses do not participate in radd in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل دخل على مريض فقال له أوصي فقال لماذا أوصي فإن مالي يرثه عمتاك وخالتاك وجدتاك كيف كانت هذه قال : كان هذا المريض تزوج جدتي الرجل أم أبيه وأم أمه فولدت كل واحدة للمريض ابنتين فلما مات المريض ترك أربع بنات بنتان منهن خالتا الرجل وبنتان منهن عمتا الرجل والمرأتان هما جدتا الرجل فللبنتان الثلثان وللمرأتين الثمن وما بقي يرد على البنات إن لم يكن له عصبة.
A man entered upon a sick person and said to him: Make a will. He said: Why should I make a will? For my wealth is inherited by your two paternal aunts, your two maternal aunts, and your two grandmothers. How was this? He said: This sick person married the two grandmothers of the man: the mother of his father and the mother of his mother. Each one bore for the sick person two daughters. When the sick person died, he left four daughters: two of them are the maternal aunts of the man, and two of them are his paternal aunts. The two women are the grandmothers of the man. So for the two daughters [the aunts] two-thirds, for the two women [the grandmothers] one-eighth, and the remainder is returned to the daughters if he has no residuary heir.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents a inheritance puzzle where a man (the questioner) is told that the sick person's estate will be divided among his (the questioner's) own relatives. The explanation: the sick person married the questioner's two grandmothers (his father's mother and mother's mother). Each grandmother bore two daughters for the sick person. So the sick person leaves four daughters: two are the questioner's maternal aunts and two are his paternal aunts. The two grandmothers are the questioner's grandmothers but they are wives of the deceased. The heirs: the four daughters get two-thirds (Quranic share for two or more daughters), the two wives get one-eighth (share of multiple wives), and the remainder (after subtracting 2/3 + 1/8 = 19/24) is returned to the daughters via radd, since there is no asabah. The total shares: daughters 2/3 = 16/24, wives 1/8 = 3/24, remainder 5/24 returned to daughters, making their total 21/24 = 7/8 each? Actually equally among four daughters: each gets 21/96? The math works out. This illustrates how inheritance can include distant relatives and the principle of radd.

Key Terms
رد (radd): Return of surplus estate to Quranic heirs when no asabah exists, in proportion to their shares.
فرض (fard): Fixed Quranic share (e.g., 2/3 for daughters, 1/8 for wife).

Key Terms from Source Passage

In Hanafi faraid, when a deceased leaves only Quranic heirs and no asabah, the surplus estate is returned (radd) to the Quranic heirs proportionally, excluding spouses.

Inheritance Among Simultaneous Deaths: The Certainty Principle
Ruling with proof on simultaneous death inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that no inheritance occurs between persons who die simultaneously when the order of death is unknown, and distinguish it from cases where the order is known.

Core Idea
Inheritance requires certainty of the heir's life after the decedent's death; doubt blocks mutual inheritance.

This passage addresses a classic faraid scenario: when a group dies together in a disaster and we cannot know who died first. The Hanafi rule, supported by leading Companions, is that each estate passes to its own living heirs, with no inheritance between the deceased. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and its application through examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Identify the condition that triggers the rule: multiple persons die in a single event (drowning, burning, wall collapse, battle) and the order of death is unknown.
If the order of death is known, the later inherits from the earlier.
2
State the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: no inheritance between the deceased; the property of each goes to that person's own heirs alone.
This applies regardless of the number of deceased.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (dalil aqli)
Inheritance depends on certainty (yaqin) that the heir survived the deceased. Since the order of death is unknown, this certainty is absent, so inheritance by doubt (shakk) is invalid.
The principle: al-ibratu fi al-irth bi al-yaqin (inheritance is based on certainty).
4
Apply to examples
Walk through the given examples (two brothers, two emancipated brothers, mother and son) and see how the rule is applied to each estate separately.
The same method extends to all similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توارث بين الغرقى والحرقى إلا إذا علم ترتيب الموت. قال رحمه الله: (ولا توارث بين الغرقى والحرقى إلا إذا علم ترتيب الموت) أي إذا مات جماعة في الغرق أو الحرق ولا يدري أيهم مات أولاً جعلوا كأنهم ماتوا جميعاً فيكون مال كل واحد منهم لورثته ولا يرث بعضهم بعضاً إلا إذا عرف ترتيب موتهم فيرث المتأخر من المتقدم وهو قول أبي بكر وعمر وزيد وأحد الروايتين عن علي رضي الله عنه. وإنما كان كذلك لأن الإرث ينبني على اليقين بسبب الاستحقاق وشرطه وهو حياة الوارث بعد موت المورث ولم يثبت ذلك فلا يرث بالشك، وكذلك الحكم إذا ماتوا بهدم الجدار عليهم أو في المعركة ولا يدري أيهم مات أولاً وفي الأصل أخوان غرقاً وخلف أحدهما بنتاً وعشرين ديناراً مثلاً وخلف الآخر بنتاً وعشرة دنانير؛ فعلى قول عامة أصحابه وعامة الفقهاء للبنت النصف من المال والنصف الباقي لابن العم وما تركه الآخر لابنه. أخوان معتقان غرقا وخلف أحدهما ابناً وبنتاً وخلف الآخر بنت ابن ومولى فالذي خلف ابنة ابن ماله على قول العامة بين ابنة ابنه وبين ابن أخيه الذي غرق معه نصفان النصف لابنة الابن سهم ولا عصبة ولا يرث مع ذي سهم وعصبة سوى أحد الزوجين لعدم الرد عليهما والنصف لابن الأخ وحده امرأة وابنها غرقاً وخلفت المرأة زوجاً هو أب الابن وخلف الابن أباه وأبناً؛ فعلى قول العامة مال المرأة يقسم بين زوجها وبن ابن ابنها وللزوج الربع والباقي لابن الابن ومال الابن يقسم بين ابنه وبين الأب للأب السدس والباقي للابن، وعلى هذا القياس يخرج جنس هذه المسائل.
There is no inheritance between drowned and burned persons unless the order of death is known. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And no inheritance between drowned and burned persons unless the order of death is known) meaning: if a group dies by drowning or burning and it is not known who died first, they are treated as if they all died at the same time, so the property of each goes to his own heirs and none inherits from another unless the order of death is known, then the later inherits from the earlier. This is the saying of Abu Bakr, Umar, Zayd, and one of two narrations from Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). The reason is that inheritance is based on certainty of the cause and condition of entitlement, which is the life of the heir after the death of the testator, and that is not established, so inheritance by doubt is not allowed. The same applies if they die by a wall collapsing on them or in battle and the order of death is unknown. The original case: two brothers drowned, one left a daughter and twenty dinars, the other left a daughter and ten dinars; according to the general position of his companions and most jurists, the daughter takes half the estate, the remaining half goes to the cousin (son of uncle), and what the other left goes to his son. Two emancipated brothers drowned, one left a son and daughter, the other left a daughter and a mawla; the one who left a granddaughter: his property according to the general position is divided equally between his granddaughter and the son of his brother who drowned with him — half for the granddaughter, and half for the son of the brother. A woman and her son drowned, the woman left a husband who is the father of the son, and the son left his father and a son; according to the general position, the woman's property is divided between her husband and her grandson: the husband gets a quarter, the rest for the grandson. The son's property is divided between his son and his father: the father gets one sixth, the rest for the son. On this analogy all such cases are decided.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when a group dies simultaneously in an event like drowning or burning, and the order of death is unknown. The passage type is a ruling with proof, combining a matn statement and its commentary. The ruling is that no mutual inheritance occurs—each deceased's estate goes to his own living heirs, not to the other deceased persons. The reasoning is that inheritance requires certainty: the heir must be alive after the death of the deceased. Since the order of death is unknown, we cannot be certain that any one of them survived the others, so we treat them as all dying at the same moment. This is the position of the leading Companions Abu Bakr, Umar, and Zayd, and one narration from Ali. The commentary then gives several examples to illustrate the application: cases of two brothers, emancipated brothers, and a mother and son. In each case, the known heirs (like daughters, sons, parents, spouses) take their shares from the estate of the deceased to which they are related, but no one inherits from the other deceased due to uncertainty. The general principle is extended to any similar situation like collapsing walls or battles. The authority weight is from the established Hanafi position, which is consistent with general practice.

Key Terms
الغرقى والحرقى (al-gharqā wa al-ḥarqā): Literally 'drowned and burned' — refers to persons who die in drowning or fire incidents where the order of death is unknown.
ترتيب الموت (tartīb al-mawt): The sequence or order of death among multiple deceased persons.
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certainty; the legal standard required for establishing the right of inheritance (the heir must be known to survive the deceased).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two brothers die in a building collapse and the order of death is unknown, how does the Hanafi school treat their inheritance?
A) The older brother inherits from the younger.
B) The younger brother inherits from the older.
C) The estate of each brother goes to his own heirs separately, with no inheritance between them.
D) The estates are combined and distributed among all heirs of both brothers.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi faraid, when multiple persons die together and the order of death is unknown, no inheritance passes between them; each estate is distributed to that person's own living heirs.

What is Dhu Rahim? Terminology and Classification
Definition and classification of heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'dhu rahim' in Hanafi faraid terminology and classify heirs into three categories.

Core Idea
Dhu Rahim: a blood relative who is neither a prescribed sharer nor a residuary.

The passage opens by defining a key term in Hanafi inheritance law: Dhu Rahim (plural Dhawi al-Arham). Understanding this category is essential to identify who inherits when primary heirs are absent. The definition sets the stage for the subsequent dispute about their entitlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term
Locate the term 'Dhu Rahim' in the Arabic text. Note that it is conjoined to the earlier term 'Dhu Fard' (owner of a prescribed share).
Conjunction indicates the category is being contrasted with prescribed sharers.
2
Parse the definition
The text defines Dhu Rahim as a relative who is neither an owner of a prescribed share (sahm) nor a residuary (asaba). Break down the negation: 'not dhi sahm' and 'not asaba'.
In Hanafi terminology, 'dhu rahim' is a technical label for non-agnate blood relatives.
3
Recognize the threefold classification of heirs
The author states that all inheritors are relatives (dhu rahm in a broad sense), but they fall into three types: (1) those with a prescribed share, (2) those who are residuaries, and (3) those who are neither. The first two were covered earlier.
This classification is foundational for understanding the order of inheritance.
4
Connect to the rest of the passage
The definition leads to the third category, which is the subject of the upcoming dispute. This lesson focuses only on the definition and classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وذو رحم) وهو معطوف على قوله «وذو فرض في أول الكتاب (وهو قريب ليس بذي سهم ولا عصبة أي ذو الرحم وهو قريب ليس بوراث بفرض ولا بعصبة وهذا على اصطلاح أهل هذا العلم. وفي الحقيقة الوراث لا يخرج من أن يكون ذا رحم وتحته ثلاثة أنواع : قريب وهو ذو سهم، وقريب هو عصبة، وقريب ليس بذي سهم ولا عصبة فقدمنا الكلام في الأولين وبقي في الثالث فنقول : عندنا يرثون عند عدم النوعين الأولين وهو قول عامة الصحابة رضي الله عنهم غير زيد بن ثابت فإنه قال : لا ميراث لذوي الأرحام بل يوضع في بيت المال روي عن عطاء ابن يسار أن رجلاً من الأنصار جاء إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : يا رسول الله رجل هلك وترك عمته وخالته فسأل النبي وذلك ثلاث مرات ثم قال : لا شيء لهما. وفي بعض رواياته لا أرى ينزل على شيء لا شيء لهما . وروي أنه قال : لا أجد لهما شيئاً . وإذا لم ينزل عليه شيء لا يمكن إثباته بالرأي لأن المقادير لا يمكن إثباتها بالرأي. ولنا ما روي عن ابن عباس أن النبي آخى بين أصحابه فكانوا يتوارثون بذلك حتى نزلت وأولوا الأرحام بعضهم أولى ببعض في كتاب الله ﴾ [الأنفال : ٧٥] فتوارثوا بذلك. وعن بن معدى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: من ترك مالاً فلورثته وأنا وارث من لا وارث له أعقل عنه وأرثه، والخال وارث من لا وارث له يعقل عنه ويرثه رواه أحد وأبو داود وغيرهما . وحين مات ثابت بن الدحداح وكان غريباً لا يعرف من أين هو قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبا لبابة بن المنذر أخته فأعطاه ميراثه . وعن بن أمامة بن سهل أن رجلاً وليس له وراث إلا خالاً فكتب في ذلك أبو عبيدة إلى عمر فكتب عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : الله ورسوله مولى من لا مولى له والخال وارث من لا وارث له . وقال الترمذي : حديث حسن. وقال الطحاوي: هذا آثار متصلة قد توارثت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وعلى هذا كانت الصحابة رضي عنهم حتى روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه في عم لأم وخالة أعطى العم الثلثين والخالة الثلث. ويحتمل أن يكون هناك من هو أولى منهما أو كان ذلك قبل نزول الآية ويحتمل أن قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا شيء لهما أراد به الفرض أي لا فرض لهما مقدر
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And dhu rahm) and it is conjoined to his saying 'and dhu fard' at the beginning of the book (and it is a relative who is not an owner of a prescribed share nor a residuary). That is, dhu arham is a relative who is not an inheritor by prescribed share or by residuary, according to the terminology of the people of this science. In reality, an inheritor is not beyond being a relative, and under this are three types: a relative who is an owner of a prescribed share, a relative who is a residuary, and a relative who is neither an owner of a prescribed share nor a residuary. We have already discussed the first two, and the third remains. We say: According to us, they inherit in the absence of the first two types, and this is the saying of the generality of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) except Zayd ibn Thabit, for he said: There is no inheritance for Dhawi al-Arham, rather it is placed in the public treasury. It is narrated from Ata ibn Yasar that a man from the Ansar came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and said: O Messenger of Allah, a man died leaving his paternal aunt and maternal aunt. The Prophet asked (three times) then said: Nothing for them. In some narrations: I do not see that revelation has come down regarding anything for them. And it is narrated that he said: I do not find anything for them. And if nothing came down regarding them, it cannot be established by opinion because the shares cannot be established by opinion. Our proof is what is narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet established brotherhood between his Companions and they used to inherit by that until the verse was revealed: 'But kindred by blood are nearer to one another (in inheritance)' (Anfal 75). So they inherited by that. And from [Ibn Mas'ud?] from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: Whoever leaves property, it is for his heirs, and I am the heir of one who has no heir—I pay blood money for him and inherit from him, and the maternal uncle is the heir of one who has no heir—he pays blood money for him and inherits from him. (Narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud and others). When Thabit ibn al-Dahdah died and he was a stranger not known where he came from, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) called Abu Lubaba ibn al-Mundhir and his sister and gave him his inheritance. And from [Umamah?] ibn Sahl that a man had no heir except a maternal uncle, so Abu Ubayda wrote about that to Umar, and Umar wrote that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: Allah and His Messenger are the protector of one who has no protector, and the maternal uncle is the heir of one who has no heir. Tirmidhi said: This is a good hadith. Tahawi said: These are connected narrations that have been transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and upon this the Companions were, until it is narrated from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding a paternal half-brother (from mother) and a maternal aunt: he gave the brother two thirds and the maternal aunt one third. And it is possible that there was someone more deserving than them, or that was before the revelation of the verse. And it is possible that his saying (peace be upon him) 'nothing for them' meant the prescribed share, i.e., no prescribed share for them is determined.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses 'Dhu Rahim' (plural: Dhawi al-Arham), which refers to blood relatives who are neither Quranic sharers (ashab al-furud) nor residuaries (asaba). The author clarifies the terminology: all inheritors are relatives, but they fall into three categories: those with prescribed shares, those who take the residue, and those who are neither. The first two were covered earlier; this section deals with the third. The Hanafi position, following the majority of Companions and later Maliki and Shafi'i scholars, is that Dhawi al-Arham inherit when no prescribed sharers or residuaries exist. The sole dissenter among the Companions is Zayd ibn Thabit, who held that such relatives do not inherit and the estate goes to the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). The passage then presents the evidence for the opposing view (the hadith of the Ansari man about a paternal and maternal aunt, suggesting no inheritance) and counters it with hadith proofs supporting inheritance: (1) the verse from Surah al-Anfal indicating that blood relatives are closer, (2) the hadith of the Prophet saying the maternal uncle is the heir of one with no heir, (3) the practice of the Prophet in the case of Thabit ibn al-Dahdah, and (4) the correspondence between Abu Ubayda and Umar. The commentary also provides possible reconciliations: the hadith about 'nothing for them' may refer to the absence of a prescribed share for those specific relatives, not the total denial of inheritance. The authority weight of the Hanafi position is supported by multiple hadith and the practice of the early Caliphs.

Key Terms
ذو رحم (dhū raḥim): A blood relative who is neither an inheritor by prescribed share (fard) nor by residuary (asabah).
عصبة (ʿaṣabah): A residuary heir who takes the remainder after prescribed shares are distributed, or the whole estate if no prescribed sharers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which of the following is a dhu rahim?
A) Son's son (paternal grandson) when no son exists.
B) Maternal uncle.
C) Wife (if no children).
D) Mother.

Key Takeaway
Dhu rahim is a blood relative who inherits only when no prescribed sharers or residuaries exist.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate

The Hanafi Position on Inheritance of Dhawi al-Arham
Inter-madhhab dispute on inheritance of non-agnates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on Dhawi al-Arham inheritance, cite the key proofs, and evaluate the opposing view of Zayd ibn Thabit.

Core Idea
Dhawi al-Arham inherit according to the majority of Companions and the Hanafi school, contra Zayd ibn Thabit.

After defining Dhawi al-Arham, the author presents the inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that these relatives inherit in the absence of primary heirs, supported by multiple hadith and the practice of the early Caliphs. The sole dissenter among the Companions is Zayd ibn Thabit.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The text explicitly says: 'According to us, they inherit in the absence of the first two types.' Identify this as the Hanafi view, which is also that of the majority of Companions.
Note the phrase 'عندنا' (according to us) anchors the school's position.
2
Identify the opposing view
The text mentions Zayd ibn Thabit as the exception: he held that Dhawi al-Arham do not inherit; the estate goes to Bayt al-Mal.
This is a minority view among the Sahaba.
3
Examine the opposing proof
Read the hadith of the Ansari man who left a paternal aunt and maternal aunt. The Prophet said 'Nothing for them.' This is the primary evidence for Zayd's position.
Note that the hadith is narrated by Ata ibn Yasar.
4
Examine the Hanafi proofs
The text provides several proofs: (1) the verse from Surah al-Anfal (75): 'But kindred by blood are nearer...', (2) the hadith 'The maternal uncle is the heir of one who has no heir', (3) the case of Thabit ibn al-Dahdah, and (4) Umar's practice.
These are multiple ahadith (zanni thubut) but strong dalala.
5
Understand the reconciliation
The author suggests that the hadith 'Nothing for them' may mean no prescribed share, not total denial of inheritance. This reconciles the apparent conflict.
This is a key hermeneutical move.

Commentary

عصبة (ʿaṣabah): A residuary heir who takes the remainder after prescribed shares are distributed, or the whole estate if no prescribed sharers.
بيت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Islamic state, which receives estates when there are no eligible heirs.

Faraid — Inheritance

Inheritance of Distant Kindred with Spouses
Statement of ruling when distant kindred inherit alongside spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which distant kindred (dhawi al-arham) inherit and explain the legal rationale based on the absence of radd for spouses.

Core Idea
Distant kindred inherit only with a spouse because spouses cannot receive surplus via radd.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi rule on when distant kindred may inherit. The general principle is that distant kindred are excluded by any fixed-share heir (ashab al-furud) or agnatic heir (asaba). However, there is one exception: when the only fixed-share heir is a spouse, the distant kindred do inherit, because the spouse does not receive radd (return of the surplus). This ruling is based on a talil (causal reasoning) that spouses have no blood kinship with the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify General Rule and Exception
The general rule is that distant kindred do not inherit when any fixed-share heir or agnatic heir exists. The exception is when the only fixed-share heir is a spouse. Identify this exception and note that the spouse can be either husband or wife.
The Arabic phrase 'سوى أحد الزوجين' means 'except one of the two spouses'.
2
Understand the Rationale: Absence of Radd
Spouses do not receive radd (return of surplus) because they are not blood relatives. Therefore, after the spouse takes their prescribed share, the remaining surplus goes to distant kindred. Contrast this with other fixed-share heirs (e.g., daughter) who do receive radd and thus exclude distant kindred.
Radd is a Hanafi concept not accepted by all schools.
3
Recognize the Historical Basis
The ruling is attributed to the generality of the Companions (al-ammah), including Caliph Uthman. This ijma (consensus) strengthens the authority of the Hanafi position.
The text says 'وعليه عامة الصحابة' meaning 'upon this are the generality of the Companions'.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i Position
The Shafi'i school does not allow distant kindred to inherit at all; the surplus goes to the public treasury (bayt al-mal) when there is no asaba. This is a key cross-madhhab difference.
This ikhtilaf stems from different views on the hadith: 'I leave no heirs; what I leave is sadaqah' and the definition of qarabah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولا يرث مع ذي سهم وعصبة سوى أحد الزوجين لعدم الرد عليهما) أي لا يرث ذوو الأرحام مع وجود ذوي فرض أو عصبة إلا إذا كان صاحب الفرض أحد الزوجين فيرثون معه لعدم الرد عليه لأن العصبة أولى. وكذا الرد على ذي السهام أولى من ذوي الأرحام لأنهم أقرب إلا الزوجين وأنهما لا قرابة لهما مع الميت فلهذا يرد عليهما ما فضل من فرضهما وعليه عامة الصحابة، وكان عثمان الزوجين أيضاً وقد عرف في موضعه
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: And none inherits with a possessor of a share and an agnatic heir except one of the two spouses, due to the absence of return upon them. That is, the distant kindred do not inherit with the existence of an owner of a fixed share or an agnatic heir, unless the owner of the fixed share is one of the two spouses, then they inherit with him due to the absence of return upon him, because the agnatic heir is more entitled. Likewise, the return upon the owner of shares is more entitled than the distant kindred because they are closer, except the two spouses, and because they have no kinship with the deceased, so for this reason the surplus of their fixed share is returned to them. And upon this are the generality of the Companions, and Uthman also returned to the two spouses, and it has been known in its place.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on when distant kindred (dhawi al-arham) inherit. Distant kindred are relatives who are neither ashab al-furud nor asabat. The general principle is that they do not inherit when any fixed-share or agnatic heir exists, except spouses. Spouses do not receive radd (return) of the surplus, so the surplus goes to distant kindred. The Companions including Uthman practiced this.

Key Terms
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives who are neither ashab al-furud nor asaba, such as children of daughters, maternal uncles, etc.
الرد (al-radd): Return of surplus estate to fixed-share heirs when shares do not exhaust it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do distant kindred inherit with a spouse but not with a daughter regarding the surplus?
A) Because a daughter is an agnatic heir
B) Because a spouse does not receive radd
C) Because a daughter can be excluded by a son
D) Because the spouse has a smaller share

Key Takeaway
Distant kindred inherit alongside a spouse because spouses have no blood kinship and thus do not receive radd.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
8 min
Order of Priority Among Distant Kindred
Explanation of five categories of distant kindred and a report of Abu Hanifa's retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five categories of distant kindred (dhawi al-arham) and state the mufta bihi position that children of daughters take precedence over the pseudo-grandfather.

Core Idea
Distant kindred inherit in an order mirroring asabat, and the final fatwa gives children of daughters priority over the pseudo-grandfather.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains how distant kindred (dhawi al-arham) are ranked for inheritance, analogous to the asabat system. It also records an important internal Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa initially gave precedence to the pseudo-grandfather over children of daughters, but he later retracted and agreed with the Sahibayn that children of daughters are closer. The relied-upon fatwa today follows this retracted view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Categories
First, note that the passage presents two enumerations: a four-category system (branches, roots, branches of parents, branches of grandparents) and a more detailed five-category system from al-Uthmani. List these five categories in order.
The five categories are: (1) children of daughters, (2) pseudo-grandfathers and pseudo-grandmothers, (3) children of sisters etc., (4) uncles/aunts through mothers, (5) more distant relatives.
2
Understand the Analogy to Asabat
The order is said to 'kataratib al-asabat' – meaning the closest in relation (branch before root, etc.) inherits first. This mirrors the rule for asabat (agnatic heirs) but applies to maternal and female-linked relatives.
The principle: the nearer in descent/relation has stronger entitlement.
3
Isolate the Dispute
The key dispute: Who is more entitled – the pseudo-grandfather (e.g., mother's father) or children of daughters (e.g., daughter's son)? Abu Hanifa initially favored the pseudo-grandfather; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad favored children of daughters.
The pseudo-grandfather is an 'asl' (root), while children of daughters are 'far'' (branch).
4
Trace the Retraction
The text reports that Abu Hanifa retracted to the view of the Sahibayn. This retraction is recorded in al-Zahiriyyah and is the basis for the mufta bihi.
The mufta bihi is that children of daughters take precedence over the pseudo-grandfather.
5
Apply the Mufta Bihi
In a practical case, if a deceased leaves a daughter's son and a mother's father, the daughter's son inherits first. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The retraction is an important example of a senior mujtahid changing his view based on evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وترتيبهم كترتيب العصبات) يعني ترتيب ذوى الأرحام في الإرث كترتيب العصبات يقدم فروع الميت كأولاد البنات وإن سفلوا، ثم أصوله كالأجداد الفاسدين والجدات الفاسدات وإن علوا، ثم فروع أبويه كأولاد الأخوات وبنات الأخوة وبني الإخوة لأم وإن نزلوا، ثم فرع جده وجدته كالعمات والأعمام لأم والأخوال والخالات وإن بعدوا فصاروا أربعة أصناف. وروى أبو سليمان عن محمد بن الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن أولادهم بالميراث الأصول والأول أصح لأن الفرع أقرب كما في العصبات وفي المضمرات وهم عشرة أولاد البنات وأولاد الأخوات وبنات الأخ وبنت العم والخال والخالة وأب الأم وعم الأم والعمة وولد الأخ ومن أدلى بهم. وفي العثماني وهم خمسة أصناف أولهم أولاد البنات، والثاني الجد الفاسد والجدات، والثالث أولاد الأخوات لأب وأم أو لأب وأولاد بنات الابن وأولاد الإخوة والأخوات لأم وبنات الأعمام وأولاد هؤلاء الإخوة كلهم والرابع الأعمام لأم والأخوال والخالات والعمات وبنات الأعمام وأولاد هؤلاء، والخامس عمات الأباء والأمهات كلهم وأخوالهم وخالاتهم وأعمام الآباء بالأم وأعمام اومهات كلهم وأولاد هؤلاء فأولاهم بالميراث أولهم ثم ثانيهم ثم ثالثهم ثم رابعهم ثم خامسهم وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة وعليه الفتوى، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أن الجد الفاسد أولى بالميراث من أولاد البنات وأولاد بنات الابن. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد وأولاد الأخوات وبنات الإخوة أولى من الجد الفاسد أبو الأم وكل واحد أولى من ولده أولى من أبويه عندهما وفي الظهيرية : وقد صح رجوع أبي حنيفة إلى قولهما في تقديم أولاد البنات وعليه الفتوى
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: And their order is like the order of the asabat. Meaning, the order of the distant kindred in inheritance is like the order of the asabat: the branches of the deceased precede, like the children of daughters even if they descend; then his roots, like the pseudo-grandfathers and pseudo-grandmothers even if they ascend; then the branches of his parents, like the children of sisters, daughters of brothers, and sons of brothers from the mother even if they descend; then the branches of his grandfather and grandmother, like paternal aunts, uncles from the mother, maternal uncles and aunts even if they are distant. Thus they become four categories. And Abu Sulayman narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa that their children are [inheritors?]... the first is more correct because the branch is closer as in the asabat. And in al-Mudmarat, they are ten: children of daughters, children of sisters, daughters of brothers, daughter of paternal uncle, maternal uncle, maternal aunt, grandfather from mother, paternal uncle from mother, paternal aunt, and child of brother and those who trace descent through them. And in al-Uthmani, they are five categories: first, children of daughters; second, the pseudo-grandfather and pseudo-grandmothers; third, children of sisters from father and mother or from father, and children of sons' daughters, and children of brothers and sisters from mother, and daughters of paternal uncles, and the children of all these; fourth, paternal uncles from mother, maternal uncles, maternal aunts, paternal aunts, daughters of paternal uncles, and their children; fifth, paternal aunts of fathers and mothers all, and their maternal uncles and aunts, and paternal uncles of fathers through mother, and paternal uncles of mothers all, and their children. So the first of them in inheritance is the first, then the second, then the third, then the fourth, then the fifth. And there is a narration from Abu Hanifa and upon it is the fatwa. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that the pseudo-grandfather is more entitled to inheritance than the children of daughters and children of sons' daughters. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: the children of sisters and daughters of brothers are more entitled than the pseudo-grandfather, and the father's mother. And each one is more entitled than his child, and the child is more entitled than his parents according to them. And in al-Zahiriyyah: It has been confirmed that Abu Hanifa retracted to their saying regarding giving precedence to the children of daughters, and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage explains the order of inheritance among distant kindred (dhawi al-arham). The order is analogous to asabat: branches (descendants) first, then roots (ancestors), then branches of parents, then of grandparents, yielding four categories. In al-Uthmani, five categories are detailed. There is a dispute: Abu Hanifa initially gave precedence to pseudo-grandfather over children of daughters, but he later retracted to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that children of daughters take precedence. The mufta bihi is the retracted view.

Key Terms
الجد الفاسد (al-jadd al-fasid): Pseudo-grandfather, a grandfather through a female link (e.g., mother's father).
الجدة الفاسدة (al-jaddah al-fasidah): Pseudo-grandmother, a grandmother through a female link.
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant kindred – relatives who are not asabat nor Quranic heirs with prescribed shares. They inherit in a specific order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final Hanafi fatwa, who among distant kindred is given precedence over the pseudo-grandfather?
A) The paternal uncle
B) The children of daughters
C) The maternal uncle
D) The sister's son

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school for distant kindred inheritance is that children of daughters take precedence over the pseudo-grandfather, following the retraction of Abu Hanifa.

Estate Distribution Among Distant Kindred and the Gender Dispute
Rules for dividing estate among multiple distant kindred and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for distributing an estate among distant kindred and apply the mufta bihi view when linking ancestors differ in gender.

Core Idea
When distant kindred inherit, ancestor gender determines shares, not inheritor gender — per the mufta bihi Hanafi view.

Distant kindred inherit when no primary heirs exist. This lesson covers how to divide an estate among multiple distant kindred, including the key principle of closeness and the debated role of ancestor gender. We will follow the ruling of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, which is the operative position according to the majority of later Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of distant kindred
List the five categories as given in the passage: those ending at the deceased (e.g., daughter's children), those from whom the deceased descends (false grandfather/grandmother), those descending from deceased's parents, from grandparents, and from great-grandparents. This sets the scope for distribution.
2
Apply the principle of closeness
Among distant kindred, the closest to the deceased inherits first. If equal in closeness, a child of an heir (i.e., an ancestor who is an heir) is preferred. If still equal and no child of an heir, divide equally if all same gender, or male gets double if mixed.
3
Handle equal closeness with no child of an heir
When closeness is equal and none is a child of an heir, division is based on gender of the inheritors unless the gender of the linking ancestors differs, which triggers the dispute.
4
Recognize the dispute when ancestor gender differs
If the linking ancestors (usul) differ in gender, Abu Yusuf considers the gender of the actual inheritors (branches), while Muhammad considers the gender of the ancestors. The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view, supported by Abu Hanifa.
5
Apply the mufta bihi view
Use Muhammad's method: assign shares based on ancestor gender (males get double females in the ancestor generation), then pass down to descendants. For example, a male through a female ancestor and a female through a male ancestor receive shares reflecting the ancestor's gender.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the ruling concerning them is that if one of them is alone, he deserves the entire property, and this is because the distant kindred inherit by way of agnatic relationship from one aspect... In summary, they are of five categories... And in al-Kafi: they agreed that distant kindred are not excluded by the husband and wife, meaning they inherit with them... If equal and no child of heir, divide equally if all same gender, or male gets double if mixed. If roots differ, according to Abu Yusuf, consider bodies of branches: divide equally if same gender, or male double if mixed. Then what falls to each side goes to his children. Explanation: if leaves daughter's daughter (female) and son of daughter (male), money between them with male getting double; likewise if leaves son of son's daughter and daughter's daughter's daughter, money between them male double. And if leaves daughter's daughter's daughter and son's daughter... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage details the distribution of the estate among multiple distant kindred. Key points: (1) A single distant kindred takes all. (2) They are classified into five categories based on lineage route. (3) Spouses do not block them; spouse takes share, rest goes to distant kindred. (4) Within a category, closeness determines priority; if equal closeness, child of an heir (one whose parent is an heir) is preferred; if still equal and no child of heir, division is equal for same gender or male double for mixed. (5) If the gender of the linking ancestors (usul) differs, there is an ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf considers the gender of the actual inheritors (branches), while Muhammad considers the ancestors, and the mufta bihi is Muhammad's view.

Key Terms
البطن (al-batn): Side or line of descent; a group of descendants from a common ancestor.
الأصول (al-usul): Ancestors through whom the inheritance reaches the distant kindred.
الفروع (al-furu'): Descendants of the ancestors, i.e., the actual inheritors.

Key Takeaway
When distant kindred inherit and their linking ancestors differ in gender, the Hanafi mufta bihi view distributes shares based on ancestor gender, not inheritor gender.

Definition and Categories of Dhawu al-Arham
Definition and categorization of dhawu al-arham with inheritance rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dhawu al-arham, list their five categories, and apply the priority rules for inheritance within the first category.

Core Idea
Dhawu al-arham inherit after Quranic heirs and agnates; priority depends on closeness and descent from heirs.

The category of dhawu al-arham (uterine relatives) completes the inheritance system. While Quranic heirs and agnates have fixed shares or priority, dhawu al-arham inherit in their absence. This passage defines them, classifies them into five groups, and establishes key rules for distribution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Identify that dhawu al-arham are relatives without fixed shares and not agnates from every perspective, but they are considered agnates from one aspect because they inherit by resemblance to agnates.
Their right arises from kinship, not from a prescribed share.
2
Memorize the five categories
Learn each category based on how they connect to the deceased: (1) those ending with the deceased (e.g., children of daughters), (2) those to whom the deceased traces (false grandparents), (3) those tracing to the deceased's parents, (4) those tracing to the deceased's grandparents, (5) those tracing to the grandparents of the deceased's parents.
Category mapping: relative position to the deceased determines priority.
3
Learn the spouse rule
Understand that dhawu al-arham are not excluded by the husband or wife; they inherit alongside the spouse, taking the residue after the spouse's share.
This is a consensus among Hanafi jurists.
4
Apply priority rules for the first category
The first category (those connecting directly) is prioritized by closeness to the deceased. If equal in closeness, the one who is a child of an heir (i.e., whose parent was a Quranic heir or agnate) takes precedence. If still no distinction, property is divided equally; if mixed genders, males get twice the share of females (when the ancestors' genders are consistent).
The rule of child-of-heir applies only when closeness is equal; otherwise closeness overrides.
5
Practice with the example
Work through the example: husband, daughter's daughter, maternal aunt, paternal cousin's daughter. The husband gets half, and the daughter's daughter gets the residue because she is the closest in the first category.
Daughter's daughter is a child of a Quranic heir (daughter), which also strengthens her claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون له سهم بالقرابة من الميت وليس لهم سهم مقدر والعصبة من كل وجه ذكر يدلي بعصبة ذكر ولا مقدر ففي حق ذوي الأرحام إذا لم توجد الذكورة والإدلاء إلى الميت بعصبة ذكر وجد المعنى الآخر أنه وهو قريب ليس له سهم مقدر وكانوا عصبة من وجه فيعتبر بمن يرث بالتعصيب من كل وجة أن يستحق جميع المال إذا انفرد وكذا هنا. وهم في الحاصل أصناف صنف ينتهي إلى الميت وهو الساقط من ولد الولد وإنما اعتبرنا بالساقط لأن ولد الولد على ضربين ثابت وهو من جملة أصحاب الفرائض وهو بنت الابن أو هو من جملة العصبات وهو ابن الابن وساقط هو داخل في جملة ذوي الأرحام وهو ولد البنت ذكراً كان أو أنثى، وصنف ينتمي إليه الميت كالجد الفاسد والجدة الفاسدة، وصنف ينتمي إلى أبوي الميت كبنات الإخوة لأب وأم أو لأب وأولاد الأخوات كلها، وصنف ينتمي إلى جدي الميت كالأعمام لأب وأم أو لأب، وصنف ينتمي إلى أبوي جدي الميت وهو أعمام الأب وعماته وأخواله وخالاته وأعمام الأم كلهم وعماتها وأخوالها وخالاتها وأولادهم. وفي الكافي وأجمعوا على أن ذوي الأرحام لا يحجبون بالزوج والزوجة أي يرثون معها فيعطى الزوج أو الزوجة نصيبه ثم يقسم الباقي بين ذوي الأرحام كما ستعرفه مثاله : زوج وبنت بنت وخالة وبنت عم فللزوج النصف والباقي لبنت البنت . وأما الكلام في الصنف الأول فأولاهم بالميراث أقربهم إلى الميت حتى كانت بنت البنت أولى من بنت بنت البنت، فإن استووا في القرب فمن كان ولد الوارث فهو أولى مثاله : إذا ترك بنت بنت بنت وبنت بنت ابن فالمال لبنت بنت الابن لأن أمها وارثة وكذلك إذا ترك ابن ابن بنت وبنت بنت ابن فالمال لبنت بنت الابن كما ذكرنا وإن كان أحدهما أقرب والآخر ولد الوارث لا يكون أولى. وفي الذخيرة في أصح القولين حتى إنه إذا ترك بنت بنت البنت وبنت بنت ابن ابن كان بنت بنت البنت أولى لكونها أقرب وإن استووا في القرب وليس فيهم ولد الوارث فالمال يقسم بينهم بالسوية، وإن كانوا ذكوراً كلهم أو إناثاً كلهم وإن كانوا مختلطين فللذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين وهنا بلا خلاف إذا اتفق صفة الأصول في الذكورة والأنوثة أعني بالأصول الآباء والأمهات واتفق صفة ابدان الفروع في الذكورة والأنوثة.
He has a share by kinship from the deceased and they have no fixed share. The agnate from every aspect is a male who connects through a male agnate and has no fixed share. So regarding dhawu al-arham when masculinity and connection to the deceased through a male agnate are absent, the other meaning is found: that he is a relative with no fixed share and they are agnates from one aspect, so analogy is made to one who inherits by agnation from every aspect: that he deserves all the property if alone. And so here. In summary, they are categories: a category that ends with the deceased, which is the excluded [heir] from children of offspring; a category to which the deceased traces, like the false grandfather and false grandmother; a category that traces to the deceased's parents, like daughters of full or consanguine brothers and all children of sisters; a category that traces to the deceased's grandparents, like full or consanguine paternal uncles; a category that traces to the grandparents of the deceased's parents, which are paternal uncles and aunts, maternal uncles and aunts of the father, and all paternal uncles and aunts, maternal uncles and aunts of the mother, and their children. In Al-Kafi: they agreed that dhawu al-arham are not excluded by the husband or wife, i.e., they inherit with them; so the husband or wife is given his/her share, then the remainder is divided among dhawu al-arham as you will know. Example: husband, daughter's daughter, maternal aunt, and paternal cousin's daughter; the husband gets half, the remainder goes to the daughter's daughter. As for discussion of the first category: the most entitled among them is the closest to the deceased, such that a daughter's daughter is more entitled than a daughter's daughter's daughter. If they are equal in closeness, then whoever is the child of an heir is more entitled. Example: if he leaves a daughter's daughter's daughter and a daughter's son's daughter, the property goes to the daughter's son's daughter because her mother is an heir. Similarly, if he leaves a son's son's daughter and a daughter's son's daughter, the property goes to the daughter's son's daughter as we mentioned. If one is closer and the other is a child of an heir, it does not make him more entitled. In Al-Dhakhira in the soundest of the two sayings: even if he leaves a daughter's daughter's daughter and a daughter's son's son's daughter, the daughter's daughter's daughter is more entitled due to being closer. If they are equal in closeness and among them is no child of an heir, the property is divided among them equally, whether all are males or all females. If they are mixed, then for the male the share of two females. And here there is no disagreement if the quality of the origins is consistent in masculinity and femininity, meaning by origins the fathers and mothers, and the quality of the bodies of the branches is consistent in masculinity and femininity.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage defines dhawu al-arham (uterine relatives) and their categories according to the Hanafi school. It begins by explaining that dhawu al-arham are relatives who do not have a prescribed share (like those of the Quranic heirs) and are not agnates (asabah) from every perspective, but they are considered as agnates from one perspective because they are relatives with no fixed share and inherit by resemblance to agnates. The text then categorizes them into five groups based on how they connect to the deceased: (1) those who connect directly to the deceased, like children of daughters (the 'excluded' from the category of children of offspring); (2) those to whom the deceased traces, like false grandfathers and grandmothers; (3) those who trace to the deceased's parents, like daughters of brothers and children of sisters; (4) those who trace to the deceased's grandparents, like paternal uncles; (5) those who trace to the grandparents of the deceased's parents, like paternal and maternal aunts and uncles and their children. Next, the passage states a consensus that dhawu al-arham are not excluded by the spouse; they inherit alongside the spouse, taking the remainder after the spouse's share. An example is given: a husband, a daughter's daughter, a maternal aunt, and a paternal cousin's daughter. The husband gets half, and the daughter's daughter gets the rest because she is the closest. The text then explains priority rules within the first category: the closest in degree takes precedence; if equal in degree, then the one who is a child of an heir (i.e., whose parent was a Quranic heir or agnate) is preferred. If both are equal and neither is a child of an heir, they divide equally. When genders are mixed, the rule is that a male gets twice a female's share, and there is no disagreement on this when the 'origins' (the connecting ancestors) are consistent in gender, i.e., when the series of ancestors are all males or all females in the same way.

Key Terms
ذوي الأرحام (dhawu al-arham): Relatives who are not entitled to a prescribed share nor are they agnates, but inherit through connection via females or distant males. They are categorized into five groups based on their connection to the deceased.
عصبة من وجه (asabah min wajh): A relative who is like an agnate from one perspective: they do not have a prescribed share but inherit as residual heirs, similar to asabah, due to their kinship connection.
الأصول (al-usul): Here, 'origins' refer to the connecting ancestors (parents) between the deceased and the dhawu al-arham claimants. Specifically, the gender of these ancestors (male or female) determines the method of division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given (زوج وبنت بنت وخالة وبنت عم), who receives the residue after the husband's half?
A) The maternal aunt
B) The paternal cousin's daughter
C) The daughter's daughter
D) All three equally

Key Takeaway
Dhawu al-arham inherit in the absence of agnates; their distribution follows closeness and preference for children of heirs, and they are not excluded by the spouse.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dividing Dhawu al-Arham When Ancestors' Genders Differ
Intra-Hanafi dispute on division method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's methods for dividing dhawu al-arham inheritance when the gender of connecting ancestors differs, and apply each method to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Two Hanafi masters differ: Abu Yusuf counts the living bodies; Muhammad counts the ancestors' genders.

When distributing inheritance among dhawu al-arham, a key question arises when the chain of ancestors differs in gender. This lesson unpacks the rival positions of Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, their practical consequences, and which view prevails in fatwa. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the dispute with several detailed examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What exactly is the disagreement? When the gender of the connecting ancestors (usul) differs, do we look at the gender of the present claimants (abdan) or the gender of their immediate ancestors?
Abu Yusuf considers the bodies (abdan); Muhammad considers the origins (usul).
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's method
Abu Yusuf's view: consider the living individuals. Divide the estate equally if all are same gender, or for each male twice a female's share if mixed. Then each branch (batin) passes its share to its children.
He initially agreed with Muhammad then retracted.
3
Understand Muhammad's method
Muhammad's view: imagine the estate is inherited by the immediate ancestors (e.g., son's daughter, daughter's daughter). Divide among them according to their gender (male twice female). Then each ancestor's share passes to their own children.
This requires more computation but is the more famous narration from Abu Hanifa.
4
Work through the key example
Consider: deceased leaves a daughter's daughter's daughter and a son's daughter's daughter. Apply both methods: Abu Yusuf gives each half (both female); Muhammad gives 2/3 to the son's daughter's daughter and 1/3 to the other.
This shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text states Muhammad's view is the more famous narration from Abu Hanifa and fatwa is upon it for all dhawu al-arham. However, Imam al-Asbijabi and Bukhara scholars preferred Abu Yusuf's view for its ease.
Both views have weight; the mufta bihi is Muhammad's.

ولديه أثلاثاً ثلثاه لابنه وثلثه لبنته وما أصاب بنت البنت يقسم بين ولديها أثلاثاً أيضاً ثلثه لبنتها وثلثاه لابنها فتكون القسمة تسعة . من وفي الكافي : ولو ترك بنتي ابن البنت وابن بنت بنت عند أبي يوسف ظاهر، وعند محمد يقسم أخماساً خمس المال لابن بنت البنت وأربعة أخماسه لبنتي ابن البنت كأنه مات عن ابني بنت وبنت بنت فما أصاب بنت البنت فلولدها وما أصاب الابن فلولده. ولو ترك ابني بنت بنت بنت وبنت ابن بنت بنت وبنتي بنت ابن بنت فعند أبي يوسف المال بين الفروع أسباعاً باعتبار أبدانهم، وعند محمد يقسم المال في البطن الثاني أسباعاً باعتبار عدم الفروع الأصول إذ أربعة أسباعه لبنتي بنت ابن البنت نصيب أحدهما وثلاثة أسباعه وهو نصيب البنتين يقسم على ولديهما في البطن الثالث أيضاً فنصفها لبنت ابن البنت نصيب أبيها والنصف الآخر لابني بنت بنت البنت نصيب أمها وتصح من ثمانية وعشرين، وقول محمد أشهر الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة في جميع ذوي الأرحام وعليه الفتوى. وقال الأمام الأسبيجابي في المبسوط : قول أبي يوسف أصح لأنه أسهل ولو ترك ولدي بنت بنت وبنت ابن بنت فعلى قول أبي يوسف المال بينهم باعتبار أبدانهم على أربعة أسهم سهم بنت ابن البنت وثلاثة أسهم لولدي بنت البنت سهمان للابن وسهم للبنت . وعلى قول محمد القسم باعتبار الآباء يجعل كأنه مات عن ابن بنت وعن بنت بنت فيقسم المال بينهم أثلاثاً ثلثاه لابن البنت وثلثه لبنت البنت، ثم ما أصاب ابن البنت يسلم لولده وما أصاب بنت البنت يقسم بين ولديها أثلاثاً الثلثان للابن والثلث للبنت فيحتاج إلى حساب يقسم ثلثه أثلاثاً وأقل ذلك تسعة، وعلى هذه المسائل. ومشايخ بخارى أخذوا بقول أبي يوسف في جنس هذا القياس يخرج جنس المسائل
If the quality of the origins differs, then according to Abu Yusuf's view, the bodies of the branches are considered, and the property is divided among them equally if they are all males or all females, and if mixed, for the male the share of two females. According to him, then what each branch gets goes to its children. And Abu Yusuf initially used to say as Muhammad said, then he retracted and said as we mentioned. Shaykh al-Islam Khwahrizade and most of our shaykhs place the saying of Abu Hanifa with Muhammad's saying, and other shaykhs said about Abu Hanifa in this two narrations. Exposition of these cases: if he leaves a daughter's daughter and a son's daughter, the property between them is for the male the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a son's son's daughter and a daughter's daughter's daughter, the property between them is for the male the share of two females. If he leaves a daughter's daughter's daughter and a son's daughter's daughter, according to Abu Yusuf the property between them is halves, considering their bodies. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), he divides between them into thirds: two-thirds to the son's daughter's daughter and one-third to the daughter's daughter's daughter, considering their origins. As if he died leaving a son's daughter and a daughter's daughter, and two children of a son's daughter. According to Abu Yusuf, the property among them based on bodies is into six: each male two shares, each female one share. According to Muhammad, he divides based on parents, making it as if he left a daughter's daughter and a son's daughter, so two-thirds of the property for the son's daughter and one-third for the daughter's daughter. Then what the son's daughter got is divided among her two children into thirds: two-thirds for her son and one-third for her daughter. And what the daughter's daughter got is divided among her two children also into thirds: one-third for her daughter and two-thirds for her son. So the division becomes nine. In Al-Kafi: if he leaves two daughters of a son's daughter and a son of a daughter's daughter, according to Abu Yusuf it is apparent. According to Muhammad, he divides into fifths: one-fifth for the son of the daughter's daughter and four-fifths for the two daughters of the son's daughter, as if he died leaving two sons of a daughter and a daughter's daughter: what the daughter's daughter got goes to her children, and what the son got goes to his children. If he leaves two sons of a daughter's daughter's daughter and a daughter of a son's daughter's daughter and two daughters of a daughter's son's daughter, according to Abu Yusuf the property among the branches is into sevenths considering their bodies. According to Muhammad, he divides the property in the second tier into sevenths considering the absence of branches from the origins: four-sevenths for the two daughters of the daughter's son's daughter as the share of one of them, and three-sevenths which is the share of the two daughters is divided among their two children in the third tier also: half of it for the daughter of the son's daughter as the share of her father, and the other half for the two sons of the daughter's daughter's daughter as the share of their mother. It becomes correct from twenty-eight. And the saying of Muhammad is the more famous of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa regarding all dhawu al-arham, and fatwa is upon it. Imam al-Asbijabi said in Al-Mabsut: Abu Yusuf's saying is more correct because it is easier. If he leaves two children of a daughter's daughter and a daughter of a son's daughter, according to Abu Yusuf the property among them based on bodies is into four shares: one share for the daughter of the son's daughter and three shares for the two children of the daughter's daughter: two shares for the son and one share for the daughter. According to Muhammad, the division based on parents makes it as if he died leaving a son's daughter and a daughter's daughter, so he divides the property among them into thirds: two-thirds for the son's daughter and one-third for the daughter's daughter. Then what the son's daughter got goes to her children, and what the daughter's daughter got is divided among her two children into thirds: two-thirds for the son and one-third for the daughter. So it requires a calculation dividing one-third into thirds, the least of which is nine. And upon these cases the general rule is based. The scholars of Bukhara adopted Abu Yusuf's view in this type of analogy to derive the general category of cases.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the method of dividing inheritance among dhawu al-arham when the gender of the connecting ancestors (the 'origins') differs. The dispute arises when the series of ancestors leading to the deceased are not consistent in gender. For example, one claimant may descend through a male ancestor and another through a female ancestor. The text explains two views. According to Abu Yusuf, one looks at the 'bodies' of the present claimants (i.e., the actual individuals). If all claimants are male, they divide equally; if all female, equally; if mixed, males take twice females' share. Whatever each 'branch' (batin) receives is then distributed among its own children (the next tier). According to Muhammad, one looks at the genders of the immediate ancestors of the claimants (the 'origins'). Specifically, one imagines the estate as if it were inherited by those ancestors (the parents of the claimants), distributes accordingly, and then each ancestor's share passes to their respective children. The text notes that Abu Yusuf initially held the same view as Muhammad but later retracted. Most early Hanafi scholars (like Khwahrizade) considered Abu Hanifa's view to align with Muhammad, while others report two narrations from Abu Hanifa. Several detailed examples are given to illustrate the two methods and their differing results. The text concludes that Muhammad's view is the more famous narration from Abu Hanifa for all dhawu al-arham and is the basis for fatwa, though Imam al-Asbijabi considered Abu Yusuf's view easier and some Bukhara scholars adopted it.

Key Terms
الأصول (al-usul): The connecting ancestors (parents) of the dhawu al-arham claimants. Their gender determines the division according to Muhammad's view.
الأبدان (al-abdan): The actual bodies of the claimants (i.e., the individuals themselves). Their gender and number determine the division according to Abu Yusuf's view.
بطن (batn): A tier or branch of descent. In the context, 'each branch' receives a share and then passes it to its own children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Muhammad's view
C) Abu Hanifa's view as narrated by Abu Yusuf
D) The view of the Bukhara scholars

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on Muhammad's method for dividing dhawu al-arham when the gender of ancestors differs, considering the ancestors rather than the living bodies.

Priority Among Categories of Dhawu al-Arham
Intra-Hanafi dispute on priority of dhawu al-arham categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the priority of dhawu al-arham categories after the first category and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prioritizes grandparents; the Sahibayn prioritize children of sisters and daughters of brothers.

The order of inheritance among distant relatives (dhawu al-arham) involves a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute. After the first category (children of daughters), scholars differed on which group should inherit next. This lesson examines the arguments of Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether grandparents or children of sisters and daughters of brothers have greater priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
After the first category of dhawu al-arham (children of daughters), which remaining category takes priority? The two views are grandparents (Abu Hanifa) versus children of sisters and daughters of brothers (Sahibayn).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa argues that grandparents are more entitled because dhawu al-arham inherit by agnation from one aspect (asabah min wajh). By analogy with full agnates (asabah min kulli wajh), the father takes precedence over the son's son; similarly, grandparents are like the father in rank.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning draws on the principle that the father excludes brothers, so grandparents have a stronger claim.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that children of sisters and daughters of brothers are more entitled. Children of sisters are offspring of females who receive fixed shares (sahibat fard), and daughters of brothers are offspring of agnates. Grandparents, in contrast, are not descended from any fixed-share holder or agnate.
The Sahibayn emphasize proximity to established heirs.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
If a deceased leaves a maternal grandmother, a daughter's son, and a brother's daughter, then according to Abu Hanifa the grandmother takes priority; according to the Sahibayn, the daughter's son and brother's daughter take priority before the grandmother.
The practical difference affects the actual distribution of the estate.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The text does not explicitly state which view is relied upon for fatwa. Earlier passages suggest that Muhammad's view is mufta bihi for division, but here the priority issue remains unresolved in this excerpt.
Later Hanafi practice may follow the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد الصنف الأول على قول أبي حنيفة الآخر وهو قول أبي يوسف ومحمد أي الأصناف أولى . قال أبو حنيفة الأجداد والجدات أولى. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : أولاد الأخوات وبنات الإخوة أولى لأن أولاد الأخوات أولاد صاحبات فرض وبنات الإخوة أولاد عصبة والجدات ليسوا ولد صاحب فرض ولا ولد عصبة ولا ولد ذي سهم . وأبو حنيفة يقول : ذو الأرحام يورثون على سبيل التعصيب من وجه وفي العصبات من كل وجه والجدات يرثون لأن الأب مقدم على أولاد ابنه عندي حتى أن أولاد الإخوة لأب وأم لا يرثون مع الأب عندنا فكذا في ذوي الأرحام الجدات لأم هم في درجة أب الأب لأنه يتصل.
And after the first category, according to the other saying of Abu Hanifa, which is the saying of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: which of the categories is more entitled? Abu Hanifa said: grandfathers and grandmothers are more entitled. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: children of sisters and daughters of brothers are more entitled because children of sisters are children of those who have a fixed share [females] and daughters of brothers are children of agnates, while grandmothers are neither children of a fixed-share holder nor children of an agnate nor children of someone with a share. Abu Hanifa says: dhawu al-arham inherit on the basis of agnation from one perspective, and among agnates from every perspective, grandmothers inherit because the father is given precedence over the son of his son in my view, such that the children of full or consanguine brothers do not inherit with the father with us. So likewise in dhawu al-arham, the maternal grandmothers are in the degree of the father's father because she connects.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the priority among different categories of dhawu al-arham. After the first category (children of daughters), a question arises: which of the remaining categories is more entitled? According to a second narration from Abu Hanifa (also the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the priority order is: first, children of sisters and daughters of brothers; then grandfathers and grandmothers. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that children of sisters are children of 'female fixed-share holders' (since sisters are Quranic heirs), and daughters of brothers are children of agnates, whereas grandmothers are neither. Abu Hanifa, however, prioritizes grandparents (false grandfathers and grandmothers) over children of sisters and daughters of brothers. His reasoning is that dhawu al-arham inherit on the basis of agnation from one perspective, and by analogy with agnates from every perspective, the father takes precedence over the son's son. In Hanafi inheritance, the father excludes brothers entirely. Similarly, grandparents (especially maternal grandmothers) are like the father's father in rank. Thus, grandparents inherit before children of sisters and daughters of brothers. The passage does not state which view is the mufta bihi, but it presents both positions.

Key Terms
صاحبات فرض (sahibat fard): Female relatives who are prescribed a fixed share in the Quran, such as daughters, sisters, etc. The children of such females are considered closer in the view of the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Grandfathers and grandmothers
B) Children of sisters and daughters of brothers
C) Paternal uncles and aunts
D) Children of children of daughters

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the priority of dhawu al-arham categories after the first is disputed: Abu Hanifa favors grandparents, while the Sahibayn favor children of sisters and daughters of brothers.

Priority Among Distant Ancestors: The Principle of Connection Through an Heir
An intra-Hanafi dispute on the priority rule for distant ancestors in inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute over preference among equally close distant ancestors and apply the ruled-upon position to a given inheritance scenario.

Core Idea
Among equally close distant ancestors, the one who connects through an heir is preferred, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This passage deals with inheritance rules for 'corrupt' grandfathers and grandmothers—distant ancestors not in the direct male line. When two such ancestors are equally close to the deceased, a dispute arises: does the one who traces lineage through a legitimate heir get priority? The Hanafi school has two main positions, and the relied-upon view is that of Abu Sahl al-Fara'idi. Let's examine the dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Recognize that the primary rule among distant ancestors is that the closest in lineage inherits first. This is agreed upon by all.
2
Recognize the point of dispute
When two distant ancestors are equally close, a disagreement arises: does connection through an heir (yudlī bi-wārith) grant preference? Two positions exist.
The term 'yudlī' means tracing lineage to the deceased through an intermediary.
3
State Abu Sahl's position and evidence
Abu Sahl al-Fara'idi and many mashayikh hold that the one who connects through an heir is preferred. Their evidence includes qiyas with cases like Ibn al-Akh li-Umm and Bint al-Akh li-Umm where equality leads to equal shares despite differing connections.
The fatwa of Qadi Abd al-Wahid supports this view.
4
State Abu Sulayman's position and evidence
Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani holds that connection through an heir does not give preference. He cites the case of Abu Umm al-Ab (connects through a mother) and Ab Ab al-Umm (connects through a father) where the latter connects through an heir but is not preferred.
5
Analyze the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Sahl, as per the fatwa of Qadi Abd al-Wahid. The opposing example is countered by noting that the rule is general and not negated by exceptional cases.
The concluding example in the passage shows that connecting through an heir is not always sufficient; other factors may apply.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في الأجداد الفاسدة والجدات الفاسدة فأولاهم بالميراث أقربهم إلى الميت، فإن استووا في القرب فعلى قول أبي سهل الفرائضي وجماعة من المشايخ من يدلي إلى الميت بوارث فهو أولى وفي المغرب : أدلية الدلو سلبها في البئر يدلي إلى الميت أي يتصل. وقال أبو سليمان الجرجاني : من يدلي إلى الميت بالوراث ليس بأولى بيانه: إذا مات الرجل وترك أبو أم الأب وأب أب الأم لا يدلي إلى الميت بالوراث وبه كان يفتي القاضي الأمام الشهيد عبد الواحد وعلى قول أبي سليمان ثلثا المال لأب أم الأب والثلث لأب أب الأم، وكذا إذا ترك أب الأم فعلى قول أبي سهل لا شيء لأب أب الأم والمال لأب أم الأم، وعلى قول أبي سليمان المال بينهما نصفان لأن كل واحد منهما يدلي إلى الميت بالوراثات. وذكر محمد في فرائض الأصل هذه الصورة وهو ما إذا ترك أب أم الأب وأم أم الأم، وذكر أن المال يقسم بينهما أثلاثاً ثلثاه لأب أم الأب وثلثه لأب أم الأم. قال القاضي الإمام عبد الواحد الشهيد : هذا قياس قول محمد وعلى قول أبي يوسف ينبغي أن يكون بينهما نصفان لأم أم الأم مع أب الأب إذا اجتمعتا استويا ألا ترى أن ابن الأخ لأم مع بنت الأخ لأم لا يفضل أحدهما على الآخر ولما كان لا يفضل الأخ لأم على الأخت لأم كذا هنا. ولو ترك أم أب الأم وأم أب الأب فالمال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين لأنهما يدليان إلى الميت بقرابة الأم فيقسم عليهما باعتبار أبدانها بلا خلاف كعمة لأم وعمها وخالة الأم وخالها على ما يأتي بيانه بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى فإن كان للأب الميت جد من قبل الأب أب أم أب أم كذلك يقسم المال بينهما أثلاثاً ثلثاه للجدة من قبل الأب وثلثه للجدة من قبل الأم ثم ما أصاب جدتي الأب يقسم بينهما أثلاثاً ثلثاه للجدة من قبل الأب وثلثه للجدة من قبل الأم، وهذه المسألة تدل على أن من يدلي إلى الميت بالوارث ليس بأولى فإن أب أم الأب يدلي إلى الميت بالوارث ومع هذا لا يكون أولى.
And as for the discussion about corrupt grandfathers and corrupt grandmothers, the first among them to inheritance is the closest to the deceased. If they are equal in closeness, then according to Abu Sahl al-Fara'idi and a group of mashayikh: whoever connects to the deceased through an heir is preferred. And in al-Mughrib: 'Adliyat al-dalw salbaha fi al-bi'r' – yadli ila al-mayyit meaning connects. And Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani said: whoever connects to the deceased through an heir is not preferred. Its explanation: if a man dies and leaves Abu Umm al-Ab (father of mother's father) and Ab Ab al-Umm (father of father's mother), according to Abu Sulayman: two-thirds of the wealth is for Abu Umm al-Ab and one-third for Ab Ab al-Umm, and this was the fatwa of al-Qadi al-Imam al-Shahid Abd al-Wahid. And according to Abu Sahl: nothing for Ab Ab al-Umm and the wealth goes to Ab Umm al-Umm (father of mother's mother). And according to Abu Sulayman: the wealth is between them equally because each of them connects to the deceased through inheritances. And Muhammad mentioned in Fara'id al-Asl this form: when he leaves Ab Umm al-Ab and Umm Umm al-Umm (mother of mother's mother), and he mentioned that the wealth is divided between them in thirds: two-thirds for Ab Umm al-Ab and one-third for Ab Umm al-Umm. Al-Qadi al-Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid said: This is the qiyas of Muhammad's statement. And according to Abu Yusuf, it should be half and half between them – Umm Umm al-Umm with Ab al-Ab when they meet together are equal. Do you not see that the son of a brother from the mother with the daughter of a brother from the mother does not prefer one over the other? And since the brother from the mother is not preferred over the sister from the mother, so too here. And if he leaves Umm Ab al-Umm (mother of father's mother) and Umm Ab al-Ab (mother of father's father), the wealth between them is for the male the like of the share of two females, because they both connect to the deceased through the kinship of the mother, so it is divided between them considering their bodies, without dispute, like a maternal aunt and her maternal uncle, and the maternal aunt's brother, as will be explained later if Allah wills. And if the deceased has a grandfather from the father's side, father of mother's father, similarly, the wealth is divided between them in thirds: two-thirds for the grandmother from the father's side and one-third for the grandmother from the mother's side. Then what befalls the two grandmothers of the father is divided between them in thirds: two-thirds for the grandmother from the father's side and one-third for the grandmother from the mother's side. And this issue indicates that the one who connects to the deceased through an heir is not preferred, for the father of the mother's father connects to the deceased through an heir, yet he is not preferred.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the inheritance of distant ancestors (ajdad fasida and jaddat fasida). The base ruling is that the closest in lineage inherits. When they are equal in closeness, there is a disagreement. Abu Sahl al-Fara'idi and many scholars hold that among equals, the one who traces lineage to the deceased through a legitimate heir (e.g., a son or daughter) is preferred. Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani disagrees, saying that such a connection does not grant preference. The passage provides examples to illustrate the practical outcomes of each view. For instance, if the deceased leaves a father of the mother's father (Abu Umm al-Ab) and a father of the father's mother (Ab Ab al-Umm), according to Abu Sulayman, the father of mother's father gets two-thirds and the other one-third. According to Abu Sahl, the father of father's mother gets nothing. The fatwa was given according to Abu Sahl's view by Qadi Abd al-Wahid. Later, the passage discusses a case from Muhammad al-Shaybani's Fara'id al-Asl involving Ab Umm al-Ab and Umm Umm al-Umm (mother of mother's mother), dividing the wealth in thirds, and Abu Yusuf's opinion that it should be half and half. The passage concludes with a case showing that connecting through an heir does not automatically give priority, as in the example of the father of the mother's father.

Key Terms
أجداد فاسدة (ajdād fāsida): Corrupt grandfathers – those who are not direct paternal grandfathers (e.g., through mothers).
جدات فاسدة (jaddāt fāsida): Corrupt grandmothers – those not in direct line (e.g., maternal grandmothers).
يدلي (yudlī): To connect or trace lineage to the deceased through an intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Sahl al-Fara'idi, if two distant ancestors are equally close to the deceased, who is preferred?
A) The one with closer blood relation
B) The one who traces lineage through a legitimate heir
C) The one who is older in age
D) The one who is from the father's side

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that among equally close distant ancestors, preference is given to the one who connects to the deceased through a legitimate heir, though exceptions exist.
