Inheritance of Children of Siblings: The Abu Yusuf–Muhammad Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on method of dividing dhawi al-arham shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two Hanafi methods (abdan vs usul) to calculate shares for children of siblings as dhawi al-arham, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
In dhawi al-arham inheritance, Abu Yusuf looks at the children themselves, Muhammad looks at the parents — and the difference matters.

When only children of siblings inherit as dhawi al-arham, their shares depend on whether we divide based on the children's own entitlement ('bodies') or based on what their parents would have received ('roots'). This lesson clarifies the intra-Hanafi dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine that the inheriting relatives are children of siblings (daughters of brothers, children of sisters) who are dhawi al-arham. Check if they are equal in closeness to the deceased.
The closest in lineage takes priority; if equal, then consider being a child of an heir (walad warith) for preference.
2
Apply the two methods
When the children have different parentage (e.g., from father and mother vs. from mother only), apply Abu Yusuf's method (abdan: divide directly among children) and Muhammad's method (usul: divide based on what the parents would have received).
Example: daughter of brother (from father and mother) vs daughter of brother (from father) vs daughter of brother (from mother).
3
Examine the ikhtilaf on male preference
For children of siblings from the mother side, note that Abu Hanifa treats males and females equally (no tafdhil) while Abu Yusuf gives males double.
This is a separate dispute from the abdan vs usul issue.
4
Work through examples
Use the examples in the passage: (1) daughter of brother and son of sister: Abu Yusuf gives 2/3 to son of brother and 1/3 to son of sister; Muhammad would differ. (2) Three daughters of brother: Abu Yusuf gives all to the one from father and mother; Muhammad gives 1/6 to the one from mother and rest to the one from father and mother.
These show the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Summarize the principle
The general rule for dhawi al-arham: when roots differ, division is based on roots (usul); when roots agree, division is based on bodies (abdan). Abu Yusuf argues that entitlement is through the children themselves, not the roots.
This is an application of aqli reasoning (qiyas).

وأما الكلام في أولاد الأخوات وبنات الأخوة أولاهم بالميراث أقربهم إلى الميت وفي السراجية : أولاد أولاد الأخوات لأب وأم المال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين فإن استووا في القرب، فمن كان منهم ولد وارث فهو أولى عند بعض المشايخ ومثاله : بنت بنت أخ وبنت ابن أخ فعند بعض المشايخ بنت ابن أخ أولى، وإن استووا في القرب وكان أحدهما ولد عصبة والآخر ولد صاحب فرض فعلى قول أبي يوسف الآخر يقسم المال بينهما باعتبار الأبدان، وعلى قول محمد يقسم المال بينهما باعتبار الأباء مثاله : بنت أخ وابن أخ فعلى قول أبي يوسف الثلثان لابن الأخ والثلث لابن الأخت لأنه لو ترك أخاً وأختاً توجيه قول محمد أن میرا راث ذوي الأرحام يعتبر بالأصول عند اختلاف الفروع وتعتبر بالأبدان عند اتفاق الأصول ألا ترى أنهم اتفقوا في بنت الخال وبنت العم أن للعم الثلثان وللخال الثلث وكانت هذه القسمة باعتبار أصولهما وهو الأب والأم وقالوا في العمة والعم لأم أن المال بينهما باعتبار الأبدان أثلاثاً لأن الأصول متفق، وقالوا في أولاد ذوي الأرحام عند اختلاف الأصول باعتبار الأصول وباعتبار الأبدان وأبو يوسف يقول بأن المستحق بولاء الأولاد دون الأصول فإذا اتحد جهة الاستحقاق يجب اعتبار الأبدان لا اعتبار الأصول ألا ترى أنهم قالوا في أم الأم وأم الأب إن السدس بينهما نصفان ولم يقل بأن أحدهما يدلي بقرابة الأب والآخر بقرابة الأم فيكون الثلث لقرابة الأم والثلثان لقرابة الأب لأن جهة الاستحقاق قد اختلفت لأن العمومة والخؤلة اختلف فيها جهة الاستحقاق، فإن استووا في القرب وليس فيهم ولد عصبة ولا ولد صاحب فرض فالمال يقسم بينهم على السوية إذا كانوا ذكوراً كلهم وإناثاً كلهن وإن كانوا مختلطين وقد اتفق الأصول فللذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وإن اختلف الأصول فكذلك عند أبي يوسف اعتباراً لإتيان الفروع، وعند محمد أن يعتبر أول بطن مختلف على ما ذكرنا في الصنف الأول وإن اجتمع أولاد الأخوات المتفرقات وبنات الإخوة فعند أبي يوسف من كان لأب وأم فهو أولى ممن كان لأم، وعند محمد يعتبر الأصول مثاله : إذا هلك الرجل وترك بنت أخ لأب وأم وبنت أخ لأب وبنت أخ لأم فعند أبي يوسف المال كله لبنت الأخ لأب وأم وعند محمد سدس المال لبنت الأخ لأم والباقي لبنت الأخ لأب وأم وإن اجتمع أولاد الإخوة والأخوات لأم فعند أبي حنيفة لا يفضل الذكر على الأنثى كالأصول، وعن أبي يوسف يفضل بخلاف الأصول حتى إنه لو ترك ولدي أخت لأم كانا ذكرين أو كانا أنثيين أو كان أحدهما ذكراً والآخر أنثى فالمال بينهما نصفان، وكذلك إذا ترك ولدي الأخ لأم وولدي الأخت لأم فالمال بينهم بالسوية أرباعاً. وفي السراجية : بنات الإخوة وعند أبي يوسف من كانت لأب وأم فهي أولى ممن لأب وهي أولى ممن كانت لأم. وقال محمد يعتبر الأصول
And as for the discussion about children of sisters and daughters of brothers, the first among them to inheritance is the closest to the deceased. And in al-Sirajiyyah: children of children of sisters from father and mother, the wealth between them is for the male the like of the share of two females. If they are equal in closeness, then whoever among them is a child of an heir is preferred according to some mashayikh. Its example: daughter of daughter of a brother and daughter of son of a brother. According to some mashayikh, the daughter of the son of the brother is preferred. If they are equal in closeness and one of them is a child of an agnatic heir (asaba) and the other is a child of a fixed-share heir (sahib fard), then according to Abu Yusuf: the wealth is divided between them considering the bodies, and according to Muhammad: the wealth is divided between them considering the fathers. Its example: daughter of a brother and son of a sister. According to Abu Yusuf: two-thirds for the son of the brother and one-third for the son of the sister, because if he left a brother and a sister, [the division would be different]. The elaboration of Muhammad's statement: inheritance of dhawi al-arham is based on the roots (usul) when the branches differ, and based on the bodies when the roots agree. Do you not see that they agreed in the case of the daughter of the maternal uncle and the daughter of the paternal uncle that the paternal uncle has two-thirds and the maternal uncle has one-third, and this division was based on their roots, which are the father and mother. And they said about the paternal aunt and the maternal uncle from the mother: the wealth between them is in thirds based on bodies, because the roots are alike. And they said about children of dhawi al-arham when the roots differ, based on roots and based on bodies. Abu Yusuf says that entitlement is through the children, not the roots. So when the avenue of entitlement is unified, it is necessary to consider the bodies, not the roots. Do you not see that they said about the mother's mother and the father's mother: the sixth between them is half and half, and they did not say that one of them connects through the kinship of the father and the other through the kinship of the mother, so that a third would be for the maternal kinship and two-thirds for the paternal kinship, because the avenue of entitlement is different? Because paternal uncles and maternal uncles differ in the avenue of entitlement. Then if they are equal in closeness and there is among them neither a child of an agnatic heir nor a child of a fixed-share heir, the wealth is divided between them equally if they are all males or all females, and if they are mixed and the roots agree, then for the male the like of the share of two females. And if the roots differ, then similarly according to Abu Yusuf, considering the coming of the branches. According to Muhammad, the first stomach (batn) that differs is considered, as we mentioned in the first category. And if children of different sisters and daughters of brothers gather, then according to Abu Yusuf: whoever is from father and mother is preferred over whoever is from the mother, and according to Muhammad: the roots are considered. Its example: if a man dies leaving a daughter of a brother from father and mother, a daughter of a brother from father, and a daughter of a brother from mother, according to Abu Yusuf: all wealth goes to the daughter of the brother from father and mother. According to Muhammad: one-sixth of the wealth goes to the daughter of the brother from mother, and the remainder to the daughter of the brother from father and mother. And if children of brothers and sisters from the mother gather, then according to Abu Hanifa: the male is not preferred over the female, like the roots. According to Abu Yusuf: the male is preferred, contrary to the roots. So much so that if he leaves two children of a sister from the mother, if they are both males or both females or one male and one female, the wealth between them is half and half. Similarly, if he leaves two children of a brother from the mother and two children of a sister from the mother, the wealth between them is equal in quarters. And in al-Sirajiyyah: daughters of brothers, and according to Abu Yusuf: whoever is from father and mother is preferred over whoever is from father, and is preferred over whoever is from mother. And Muhammad said: the roots are considered.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the inheritance of children of sisters and daughters of brothers (a category of dhawi al-arham). It begins by stating the general principle: the closest in lineage takes priority. If they are equal, then being a child of an heir (e.g., a child of a sibling who was an heir) may give preference according to some scholars. The core dispute is between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the method of division when the children have different parentage (e.g., from father and mother vs. from mother only). Abu Yusuf favors dividing based on the children themselves (abdan), while Muhammad considers the parents (usul). The passage provides examples, such as a case with a daughter of a brother and a son of a sister where Abu Yusuf gives two-thirds to the son of the brother and one-third to the son of the sister, while Muhammad gives a different division. The text also discusses the general rule for dhawi al-arham: when the roots (fathers/mothers) differ, the division is based on roots; when the roots agree, division is based on bodies. Abu Yusuf argues that entitlement is through the children, not the roots. The passage then covers specific scenarios: when children of siblings from the mother side inherit, Abu Hanifa holds that males and females are equal (no preference), while Abu Yusuf thinks males get double. Finally, it quotes al-Sirajiyyah on the order of preference among daughters of brothers.

Key Terms
ولد وارث (walad wārith): A child of a sibling who was an heir (i.e., the sibling died and was entitled to inherit from the deceased).
باعتبار الأبدان (bi-iʿtibār al-abdān): Considering the physical individuals (the children themselves) for division.
باعتبار الأصول (bi-iʿtibār al-uṣūl): Considering the parents (roots) – divide the estate as if the parents were inheriting, then pass to children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a woman leaving a daughter of a brother from father and mother, a daughter of a brother from father, and a daughter of a brother from mother, according to Abu Yusuf, how is the estate divided?
A) All wealth to the daughter from father and mother
B) One-sixth to daughter from mother, rest to daughter from father and mother
C) Equal shares among all three
D) Two-thirds to daughter from father and mother, one-third to the others

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two methods for dividing inheritance among children of siblings as dhawi al-arham: Abu Yusuf's method (abdan) and Muhammad's method (usul); both are valid and produce different results in cases of mixed parentage.

Inheritance of Aunts and Uncles (Dhawi al-Arham)
Rules for inheritance of aunts and uncles as dhawi al-arham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules for the inheritance of aunts and uncles as dhawi al-arham, distinguishing when they are excluded by an 'asaba and determining correct shares.

Core Idea
Paternal agnatic uncles exclude all dhawi al-arham; among aunts, closeness to the deceased determines priority.

This passage clarifies the intricate rules governing the inheritance of aunts and uncles (both paternal and maternal) when they are classified as dhawi al-arham. You will learn the priority order among different types of aunts, the effect of an agnatic heir ('asaba) excluding these distant relatives, and the principle of male taking double the female's share when only dhawi al-arham inherit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of aunts
Note the three types of aunts mentioned: from both parents, from father only, and from mother only.
This classification determines priority.
2
Determine priority among aunts
When aunts from different categories compete, the one with the strongest connection inherits. For example, an aunt from both parents takes precedence over an aunt from mother only.
The rule: 'Al-mal li-l-'amma min al-ab wa al-umm'.
3
Recognize the effect of an 'asaba
If a paternal uncle (who is an agnatic heir, 'asaba) is present, all dhawi al-arham are excluded. The wealth goes entirely to the 'asaba.
This is based on the principle: 'La mirth li-ahad min dhawi al-arham ma'a al-'asaba'.
4
Apply the male:female ratio
When only dhawi al-arham inherit and they are of the same category (e.g., all from mother), the male takes double the female's share.
This follows the general Quranic rule for inheritance.
5
Handle special cases
Note Abu Yusuf's view on division by bodies (abdan) for certain combinations, and the reference to division into thirds when both paternal and maternal sides are present.
These are nuanced details within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في الأعمام والعمات كلها والأخوال والخالات كلها يجب أن يعلم أن العمات أصناف ثلاثة : عمة لأب وأم، وعمة لأب، وعمة لأم والحكم فيهن أنه إذا كانت عمة لأب وأم وعمة لأم كان المال للعمة للأب وأم . وفي شرح الطحاوي ولو ترك عماً وعمة فإن كانا لأب وأم أو عمة وعماً لأب فالمال للعم لأنه عصبة ولا ميراث لأحد من ذوي الأرحام مع العصبة، وكذلك لو كان العم لأب وعمة لأب وأم أو لأب أو لأم فالمال كله للعم وإن كانوا جميعاً لأم فالمال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وإن ترك عمة لأب وعمة لأم كان المال كله للعمة لأب، وإن ترك عماً لأب وعمة لأب فالمال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. وكذلك إذا ترك بنت عم لأب وابن عمة لأب فالمال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وكذلك إذا ترك بنت عم لأم وابنة عم لأب قال أبو يوسف : المال بينهما يقسم باعتبار الأبدان للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. وفي الذخيرة : وإن اجتمعت قرابة الأب والأم يقسم بينهما أثلاثاً وفي شرح الطحاوي: متى اجتمع في الميراث ذو الأرحام إلا أن بعضهم أولاد العصبة وبعضهم أولاد أصحاب الفروض
And as for the discussion about all paternal uncles and aunts and all maternal uncles and aunts, it must be known that aunts are of three types: a paternal aunt from father and mother, a paternal aunt from father, and a paternal aunt from mother. The ruling concerning them is that if there is a paternal aunt from father and mother and a paternal aunt from mother, the wealth goes to the paternal aunt from father and mother. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: if he leaves a paternal uncle and a paternal aunt, if they are both from father and mother or a paternal aunt and paternal uncle from father, the wealth goes to the paternal uncle because he is an agnatic heir ('asaba), and there is no inheritance for any of the dhawi al-arham with an 'asaba. Similarly, if the paternal uncle is from father and the paternal aunt is from father and mother, or from father, or from mother, all wealth goes to the paternal uncle. And if they are all from mother, the wealth between them is for the male the like of the share of two females. And if he leaves a paternal aunt from father and a paternal aunt from mother, all wealth goes to the paternal aunt from father. And if he leaves a paternal uncle from father and a paternal aunt from father, the wealth between them is for the male the like of the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle from father and a son of a paternal aunt from father, the wealth between them is for the male the like of the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle from mother and a daughter of a paternal uncle from father, Abu Yusuf said: the wealth between them is divided considering the bodies, for the male the like of the share of two females. And in al-Dhakhirah: if the kinship of the father and mother come together, it is divided between them in thirds. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: whenever dhawi al-arham gather in inheritance, except that some of them are children of agnatic heirs and some are children of fixed-share heirs... [the text ends]
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage states the rules for inheritance of aunts and uncles (both paternal and maternal) as dhawi al-arham. It first categorizes aunts into three types based on their relation to the deceased's parents. The general rule is that the closest in lineage takes priority. When an agnatic heir ('asaba) is present, dhawi al-arham are excluded. For example, if a paternal uncle (who is an 'asaba) exists, all wealth goes to him, regardless of the presence of aunts. When only dhawi al-arham are present, the division follows specific patterns: e.g., if a paternal aunt from both parents and a paternal aunt from mother are present, the former takes all. When multiple aunts/uncles of the same category are present, the male takes twice the female's share. The passage also includes a view from Abu Yusuf on the division between a daughter of a paternal uncle from mother and a daughter of a paternal uncle from father, where division is by bodies (abdan) with double share for males. The text references Sharh al-Tahawi and al-Dhakhirah for additional details.

Key Terms
العصبة (al-ʿaṣaba): Agnatic heir who inherits the residue after fixed shares; excludes dhawi al-arham.
ذوو الأرحام (dhawū al-arḥām): Distant relatives who are not 'asaba or sahib fard; they inherit only when no primary heirs exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a deceased leaves a paternal uncle from father and a paternal aunt from father and mother, who inherits?
A) The paternal uncle gets all
B) The paternal aunt gets all
C) They split equally
D) The aunt gets two-thirds, the uncle one-third

Key Takeaway
In the absence of primary heirs, the inheritance of dhawi al-arham prioritizes proximity to the deceased, with paternal agnatic uncles excluding all others and aunts inheriting based on the strength of their relation.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad on Siblings' Children
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance among siblings' children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the methods of Abu Yusuf (considering individuals) and Muhammad (considering lineages) in distributing inheritance among children of siblings, and apply each method to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf gives priority to full-blood over half-blood by focusing on individuals; Muhammad splits the estate by considering the first differing lineage.

This passage presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute on how to distribute inheritance among children of siblings and other distant relatives (dhawi al-arham). The disagreement between the two leading students of Abu Hanifa—Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani—highlights two different approaches to resolving priority among heirs with mixed parentage. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly calculating shares in cases where full and half siblings' children coexist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the passage and note the two principles: Abu Yusuf considers the individuals (al-abdan) as the basis, giving priority to the full-blood relative. Muhammad considers the lineage chain (al-usul), specifically the first differing womb.
The dispute only arises when lineages differ; if all are full siblings' children, the standard rule applies.
2
Work through the example
The passage gives a concrete case: a man leaves three daughters of brothers (full, half through father, half through mother). Apply Abu Yusuf's method (all to full brother's daughter) and Muhammad's method (1/6 to half-through-mother, rest to full brother's daughter).
This example reveals the dramatic difference between the two methods.
3
Examine the sub-case of siblings through mother
The passage also mentions a separate dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: for children of siblings through the mother, Abu Hanifa treats males and females equally, while Abu Yusuf gives males double.
Note that Muhammad's view on this sub-case is not stated here.
4
Consider the practical impact
Reflect on how the choice of method changes the distribution. In a real fatwa, the mufti must decide which view to adopt; later Hanafi practice often favors Abu Yusuf in many faraid issues.
The passage does not declare a mufta bihi, so both remain valid positions.

Source Text

والعم [؟] لأم أن المال بينهما باعتبار الأبدان أثلاثاً لأن الأصول متفق، وقالوا في أولاد ذوي الأرحام عند اختلاف الأصول باعتبار الأصول وباعتبار الأبدان وأبو يوسف يقول بأن المستحق بولاء الأولاد دون الأصول فإذا اتحد جهة الاستحقاق يجب اعتبار الأبدان لا اعتبار الأصول ألا ترى أنهم قالوا في أم الأم وأم الأب إن السدس بينهما نصفان ولم يقل بأن أحدهما يدلي بقرابة الأب والآخر بقرابة الأم فيكون الثلث لقرابة الأم والثلثان لقرابة الأب لأن جهة الاستحقاق قد اختلفت لأن العمومة والخؤلة اختلف فيها جهة الاستحقاق، فإن استووا في القرب وليس فيهم ولد عصبة ولا ولد صاحب فرض فالمال يقسم بينهم على السوية إذا كانوا ذكوراً كلهم وإناثاً كلهن وإن كانوا مختلطين وقد اتفق الأصول فللذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وإن اختلف الأصول فكذلك عند أبي يوسف اعتباراً لإتيان الفروع، وعند محمد أن يعتبر أول بطن مختلف على ما ذكرنا في الصنف الأول وإن اجتمع أولاد الأخوات المتفرقات وبنات الإخوة فعند أبي يوسف من كان لأب وأم فهو أولى ممن كان لأم، وعند محمد يعتبر الأصول مثاله : إذا هلك الرجل وترك بنت أخ لأب وأم وبنت أخ لأب وبنت أخ لأم فعند أبي يوسف المال كله لبنت الأخ لأب وأم وعند محمد سدس المال لبنت الأخ لأم والباقي لبنت الأخ لأب وأم وإن اجتمع أولاد الإخوة والأخوات لأم فعند أبي حنيفة لا يفضل الذكر على الأنثى كالأصول، وعن أبي يوسف يفضل بخلاف الأصول حتى إنه لو ترك ولدي أخت لأم كانا ذكرين أو كانا أنثيين أو كان أحدهما ذكراً والآخر أنثى فالمال بينهما نصفان، وكذلك إذا ترك ولدي الأخ لأم وولدي الأخت لأم فالمال بينهم بالسوية أرباعاً. كانت وفي السراجية : بنات الإخوة وعند أبي يوسف من كانت لأب وأم فهي أولى ممن لأب وهي أولى ممن كانت لأم. وقال محمد يعتبر الأصول
And the paternal uncle through the mother [؟] that the wealth between them is divided by considering the individuals into thirds because the lineages are agreed. And they said regarding the children of distant relatives when the lineages differ, consideration is given to the lineages and to the individuals. Abu Yusuf says that the entitled is through the offspring, not through the lineages. So when the cause of entitlement is united, consideration must be given to the individuals, not to the lineages. Do you not see that they said regarding the mother of the mother and the mother of the father that the sixth is between them equally, and they did not say that one is connected through the father's relation and the other through the mother's relation, so that the third would be for the mother's relation and two-thirds for the father's relation, because the cause of entitlement differs? For the paternal uncles and maternal uncles, the cause of entitlement differs. If they are equal in closeness and there is no child of an ‘asaba nor a child of a fixed-share holder, then the wealth is divided equally among them if they are all male or all female. If they are mixed and the lineages agree, then the male gets double the female. If the lineages differ, it is the same according to Abu Yusuf, considering the branches. According to Muhammad, the first differing womb is considered, as we mentioned in the first category. And if the children of different sisters and daughters of brothers come together, then according to Abu Yusuf, whoever is through father and mother has priority over whoever is through mother. According to Muhammad, the lineages are considered. Example: if a man dies leaving a daughter of a brother through father and mother, a daughter of a brother through father, and a daughter of a brother through mother — according to Abu Yusuf, the entire wealth is for the daughter of the brother through father and mother. According to Muhammad, one-sixth of the wealth is for the daughter of the brother through mother, and the rest for the daughter of the brother through father and mother. And if children of siblings through the mother gather, according to Abu Hanifa the male does not surpass the female, like the lineages. From Abu Yusuf it is narrated that he surpasses, contrary to the lineages. So if he leaves two children of a sister through the mother, both male or both female or one male and one female, the wealth is between them equally. Similarly, if he leaves two children of a brother through the mother and two children of a sister through the mother, the wealth is equally among them in fourths. And in al-Sirajiyya: daughters of brothers — according to Abu Yusuf, whoever is through father and mother has priority over those through father and priority over those through mother. And Muhammad said: the lineages are considered.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the distribution of inheritance among children of siblings and other distant relatives (dhawi al-arham). The core issue is the method of calculation: Abu Yusuf considers the individuals (al-abdan) based on the number of heirs, while Muhammad considers the lineage chain (al-usul) — specifically the first differing womb. The example clearly illustrates the practical difference: when three nieces through different brothers survive, Abu Yusuf gives all to the full brother's daughter, while Muhammad gives one-sixth to the half-brother's daughter through mother and the remainder to the full brother's daughter. Abu Hanifa's separate view is noted for siblings through the mother, where he holds that males and females inherit equally, unlike the general rule for full siblings. The Sirajiyya citation resolves the rule for daughters of brothers, again showing the two jurists' differing principles.

Key Terms
ذو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant relatives who are not 'asaba (agnatic heirs) nor ashab al-furud (fixed-share heirs), e.g., children of siblings, aunts, uncles.
عصبة (‘asaba): Agnatic heir (male relative through male line) who takes the residue after fixed shares.
صاحب فرض (sahib fard): A fixed-share heir entitled to a specific Quranic fraction.
الأبدان (al-abdan): Literally 'the bodies'; here, the individual heirs as separate persons.
الأصول (al-usul): Literally 'the origins'; here, the lineage chains connecting the heirs to the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage, how does Muhammad's distribution differ from Abu Yusuf's?
A) Muhammad gives all to the full brother's daughter; Abu Yusuf gives shares to all three.
B) Muhammad gives 1/6 to the half-brother through mother's daughter; Abu Yusuf gives everything to the full brother's daughter.
C) Muhammad gives equal shares to all three; Abu Yusuf gives the full brother's daughter twice the share.
D) Abu Yusuf gives 1/3 to each; Muhammad gives 1/2 to the full brother's daughter and 1/4 to each half-brother's daughter.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on inheriting siblings' children hinges on whether to prioritize the individual (full-blood) or the lineage chain.

Inheritance Priority Among Paternal and Maternal Aunts and Uncles
Defines categories of aunts/uncles and establishes inheritance rules among dhawi al-arham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of paternal aunts and apply the two-thirds/one-third rule when paternal and maternal sides combine.

Core Idea
Paternal side takes two-thirds and maternal side one-third when both groups inherit as dhawi al-arham.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the fundamental hierarchy among aunts and uncles when they inherit as dhawi al-arham. It categorizes them by lineage and lays down the rule that the paternal side takes two-thirds when competing with the maternal side. An important internal dispute concerns whether a relative connected through both parents excludes a single-relation relative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of paternal aunts
Paternal aunts are classified as: through both parents (full), through father only, or through mother only. Same classification applies to maternal aunts/uncles.
This classification is based on the connecting link to the deceased.
2
Determine priority among paternal aunts alone
If only paternal aunts exist, the full aunt (through father and mother) takes precedence over half aunts. If both are half, the one through father is preferred over through mother.
Full blood is stronger than half blood.
3
Recognize the role of the paternal uncle as 'asaba
A paternal uncle, being a male agnate, inherits exclusively over all aunts and other dhawi al-arham, regardless of his degree of consanguinity.
This is the general rule: 'asaba excludes dhawi al-arham.
4
Apply the two-thirds/one-third split when paternal and maternal sides combine
When both paternal and maternal aunts/uncles are present, the paternal side collectively receives two-thirds, and the maternal side receives one-third. This is then subdivided within each side according to proximity and gender.
The split is based on the principle that father's relation is stronger than mother's.
5
Note the dispute on dual-relation exclusion
According to the majority (zahir al-riwaya), a relative connected through both parents (e.g., full paternal aunt) does not exclude a single-relation relative from the other side (e.g., full maternal aunt); both inherit with two-thirds/one-third. Abu Yusuf in a narration holds that the dual-relation relative excludes the single-relation one.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كانوا جميعاً لأم فالمال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وإن ترك عمة لأب وعمة لأم كان المال كله للعمة لأب، وإن ترك عماً لأب وعمة لأب فالمال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. وكذلك إذا ترك بنت عم لأب وابن عمة إحدى الطائفتين لا يحجب ذا القرابة الواحدة من الطائفة الأخرى إلا رواية عن أبي يوسف رواية ابن سماعة بيانه فيما إذا ترك عمة لأب وأم وخالة لأب وأم فالثلثان للعمة والثلث للخالة في ظاهر رواية أصحابنا . وعن أبي يوسف أن المال كله للعمة ولا شيء للخالة في ظاهر رواية أصحابنا . وأما أولاد هؤلاء فأقربهم إلى الميت أولى، وإن استووا في القرب فمن كان لأب وأم أولى ممن كان لأب، ومن كان لأب. ممن كان لأم، ومن كان يدلي إلى الميت بقرابة الأب فهو أولى ممن يدلي بقرابة الأم. وإن اختلف بطن فعند أبي يوسف يعتبر الأبدان وعند محمد يعتبر أول بطن اختلف ويقسم المال عليه نحو ما ذكرنا حتى إذا ترك بنت بنت عمة لأب وأم وابن بنت عمة لأب وأم فعلى قول أبي يوسف المال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين هذا بلا خلاف لأن الأصول قد اتفقت. وإن ترك بنت عمة لأب وأم وبنت خالة لأب وأم وبنت خالة لأب وبنت خالة لأم فلبنت العم الثلثان ولبنت الخالة الثلث والكلام في أعمام الأب لأم وعماته وأخواله وخالاته وأعمام الأم كلها وعماتها وأخوالها وخالاتها فالحكم فيهم ما ذكرنا أنه عند الانفراد أنه يستحق جميع المال وإذا اجتمعوا من جانب الأب أو من جانب الأم أو من الجانبين جميعاً فلا رواية عن أصحابنا المتقدمين واختلف المشايخ فيه والصحيح ما روي عن الحسن بن زياد وأبي سليمان الجرجاني أن الحكم فيهم كالحكم في أعمام الميت وأخواله وخالاته حتى إنه إذا اجتمع الصنفان يجعل الثلثان لقرابة الأب والثلث لقرابة الأم، ثم ما
And as for the discussion of all paternal uncles and aunts and all maternal uncles and aunts, it must be known that paternal aunts are of three types: paternal aunt through father and mother, paternal aunt through father, and paternal aunt through mother. The ruling regarding them is that if there is a paternal aunt through father and mother and a paternal aunt through mother, the wealth is for the paternal aunt through father and mother. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: if he leaves a paternal uncle and a paternal aunt, if both are through father and mother or a paternal aunt and a paternal uncle through father, the wealth is for the paternal uncle because he is an ‘asaba, and there is no inheritance for any of the dhawi al-arham in the presence of an ‘asaba. Similarly, if the paternal uncle is through father and the paternal aunt is through father and mother or through father or through mother, the entire wealth is for the paternal uncle. If all are through mother, the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females. If he leaves a paternal aunt through father and a paternal aunt through mother, the entire wealth is for the paternal aunt through father. If he leaves a paternal uncle through father and a paternal aunt through father, the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle through father and a son of a paternal aunt through father, the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle through mother and a daughter of a paternal uncle through father, Abu Yusuf said: the wealth is divided between them by considering the individuals, with the male having the share of two females. And in al-Dhakhira: if the relation from the father and the mother come together, it is divided between them in thirds. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: whenever in inheritance there are dhawi al-arham, except that some are children of ‘asaba and some are children of fixed-share holders and some are children of dhawi al-arham, then it is considered: if their degrees are different, the closest of them has priority in inheritance. If their degrees are equal, then children of dhawi al-arham do not inherit with children of ‘asaba, just as children of fixed-share holders? Actually, children of ‘asaba inherit with children of fixed-share holders. Explanation: a man dies leaving a son of his paternal cousin and a daughter of a paternal cousin; the entire wealth is for the daughter of the paternal cousin because she is from the children of ‘asaba, and the other is from children of dhawi al-arham. And if he leaves a daughter of a daughter and a daughter of a son's daughter, the entire wealth is for the daughter of the son's daughter because she is a child of a fixed-share holder. And as for maternal uncles and aunts, they are also three types: maternal uncle and aunt through father and mother, maternal uncle and aunt through father, and maternal uncle and aunt through mother. The ruling regarding them is that the first type is given precedence over the second, and the second over the third. So if he leaves a maternal uncle and aunt through father and mother, a maternal uncle and aunt through father, and a maternal uncle and a [؟] maternal uncle and aunt through mother, the wealth is between the maternal uncle and aunt through father and mother with the male having the share of two females, and nothing for the maternal uncle and aunt through father nor for the maternal uncle and aunt through mother. And if he leaves a maternal uncle and aunt through mother, the wealth is between them in thirds. And if a paternal aunt and a maternal aunt come together with a maternal uncle, two-thirds are for the paternal aunt and one-third for the maternal aunt. And if a paternal aunt through father and his maternal aunt and a paternal aunt through mother come together, two-thirds are for the father's relation and one-third for the mother's relation. Then what the father's party receives is divided among his relatives from his father's side and his relatives from his mother's side in thirds: two-thirds for the relatives from his father's side and one-third for the relatives from his mother's side. And what the mother's party receives is divided among his relatives from his father's side and his relatives from his mother's side also in thirds: two-thirds for the relatives from his father's side and one-third for the relatives from his mother's side. And a person who is related through both lines from one of the two groups does not exclude a person of single relation from the other group, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf, the narration of Ibn Sama‘a. Explanation: if he leaves a paternal aunt through father and mother and a maternal aunt through father and mother, two-thirds are for the paternal aunt and one-third for the maternal aunt in the prevailing narration from our companions. And from Abu Yusuf, the entire wealth is for the paternal aunt and nothing for the maternal aunt in the prevailing narration from our companions. And as for the children of these, the closest of them to the deceased is first. If they are equal in closeness, then whoever is through father and mother is better than whoever is through father, and whoever is through father is better than whoever is through mother, and whoever is connected to the deceased through the father's relation is better than whoever is connected through the mother's relation. And if the distances differ, according to Abu Yusuf the individuals are considered, and according to Muhammad the first differing womb is considered and the wealth is divided accordingly, as we mentioned. So if he leaves a daughter of a daughter of a paternal aunt through father and mother and a son of a daughter of a paternal aunt through father and mother, according to Abu Yusuf the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females, this without dispute because the lineages are agreed. And if he leaves a daughter of a paternal aunt through father and mother, a daughter of a maternal aunt through father and mother, a daughter of a maternal aunt through father, and a daughter of a maternal aunt through mother, then the daughter of the paternal uncle has two-thirds and the daughter of the maternal aunt has one-third. And the discussion regarding the paternal uncles of the father through mother and his paternal aunts and his maternal uncles and aunts, and all the paternal uncles and aunts of the mother and her maternal uncles and aunts: the ruling regarding them is what we mentioned: that in isolation, they are entitled to all the wealth. If they come together from the father's side or from the mother's side or from both sides, there is no narration from our early companions, and the scholars differed about it. The correct position is what is narrated from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani: that the ruling regarding them is the same as the ruling regarding the deceased's paternal uncles and aunts and maternal uncles and aunts. So if the two categories come together, two-thirds are assigned to the father's relation and one-third to the mother's relation. Then what...
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive treatment of inheritance for aunts, uncles, and their children (dhawi al-arham) according to the Hanafi school. It first categorizes paternal aunts into three types based on their relation to the deceased: through both parents, through father only, through mother only. The general rule is that the full-blood relative takes priority. For example, if a full paternal aunt and a half paternal aunt through mother exist, the full aunt gets everything. The passage then cites Sharh al-Tahawi to clarify the role of 'asaba: a paternal uncle (being a male agnate) excludes all aunts and other dhawi al-arham. Then it discusses mixed gatherings of aunts and uncles from different sides, applying the principle that when both paternal and maternal relatives are present, the paternal side receives two-thirds and the maternal side one-third. This is further subdivided recursively for those who have mixed parentage themselves. The passage also addresses the special case of 'dual relation' (dhul qarabatayn) where a relative is connected through both father and mother lines to the two groups; such a person does not exclude a single-relation relative from the other group according to the majority, but Abu Yusuf in a narration from Ibn Sama'a holds that the dual-relation relative excludes the single-relation one. Finally, it deals with children of these aunts/uncles and then extends the same principles to the aunts/uncles of the father and mother, citing al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani for the correct position.

Key Terms
ذو القرابتين (dhul qarabatayn): A relative who connects to the deceased through both father and mother lines (e.g., a full paternal aunt is related through the deceased's father and also through the father's mother? Actually, in this context, 'dual relation' means related through both father and mother of the deceased, i.e., through both parents. Example: a full paternal aunt is related to the deceased's father and also to the deceased's paternal grandmother? More accurately, as used in the text, it refers to a relative who has two links to the deceased from one of the two groups (paternal or maternal), like a full paternal aunt who is linked through the deceased's father and also through the deceased's father's mother? The text is ambiguous; in practice, it means a relative who is connected through both the father and mother of the deceased, e.g., a full sister's child would be through both parents? But here it's applied to aunts: a full paternal aunt is considered 'dual relation' because she is related through both the deceased's father and the deceased's paternal grandmother? Standard Hanafi faraid defines dhul qarabatayn as someone linked to the deceased through two paths, e.g., a full brother's daughter is linked through the brother (father's side) and also through the brother's wife? Actually, better: a relative who is related to the deceased through both the deceased's father and mother (e.g., a full sister is related through both parents; a half sister only through one). In the context of aunts/uncles, a full paternal aunt is related through the deceased's father's father and mother (i.e., both paternal grandparents), making her a dual-relation within the paternal group. Similarly, a full maternal aunt is related through both maternal grandparents. The term is used to compare a full aunt (dual) vs a half aunt (single).
بطن (batn): Level of relationship; 'different batn' means different degree of closeness (e.g., child vs grandchild). Here, when considering children of aunts/uncles, if they are at different generational levels (e.g., daughter of a daughter of a paternal aunt vs son of a daughter of a paternal aunt), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on how to divide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man dies leaving a full paternal aunt (through father and mother) and a full maternal aunt (through father and mother). According to the majority Hanafi view, what is the distribution?
A) The paternal aunt gets nothing; the maternal aunt gets everything
B) The paternal aunt gets two-thirds; the maternal aunt gets one-third
C) They share equally
D) The maternal aunt gets two-thirds; the paternal aunt gets one-third

Key Takeaway
When both paternal and maternal aunts/uncles inherit as dhawi al-arham, the paternal side receives two-thirds and the maternal side receives one-third, and a dual-relation relative does not exclude a single-relation relative from the other side.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
10 min
Inheritance for Children of Aunts and Uncles and for Extended Relatives
Defines inheritance rules for children of aunts/uncles and for extended relatives (aunts/uncles of parents)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the inheritance priority among children of aunts/uncles and apply the same two-thirds/one-third rule to aunts/uncles of the parents.

Core Idea
Closeness to the deceased determines priority among children of aunts/uncles, and the same paternal/maternal split applies to higher-level relatives.

This continuation of the passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq extends the inheritance rules to children of aunts and uncles and then to the aunts and uncles of the deceased's own parents. It clarifies that proximity (degree) is the primary factor, and when degrees are equal, full blood is preferred over half. An internal dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on how to divide when the degrees differ is also addressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the priority among children of aunts/uncles
The closest in degree to the deceased inherits first. If equal in closeness, the one through both parents (full) takes precedence over half, and within half, the one through father is preferred over through mother.
This mirrors the priority among aunts/uncles themselves.
2
Understand the dispute on differing degrees (batn)
When children of aunts/uncles are at different generational levels (e.g., a granddaughter vs. a son of a daughter), Abu Yusuf considers the individuals (the heirs themselves) and divides per capita with the male taking double the female. Muhammad considers the first differing womb (the progenitor) and divides based on that progenitor's share.
Example: In the case of a granddaughter and a grandson's daughter? The text gives: daughter of a daughter of a paternal aunt vs son of a daughter of a paternal aunt.
3
Apply the two-thirds/one-third rule to mixed children
When children of paternal aunts and children of maternal aunts are present, the paternal side collectively gets two-thirds, and the maternal side gets one-third, which is then subdivided.
Example: daughter of a paternal aunt (two-thirds) and daughter of a maternal aunt (one-third).
4
Extend the rules to aunts/uncles of the parents
The same principles apply to the aunts and uncles of the deceased's father and mother (e.g., the paternal aunt of the father through mother, etc.). When they are the sole heirs, they take the entire estate. When both sides gather, the paternal side of the father/mother gets two-thirds and the maternal side gets one-third, as per al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani.
The text notes that there is no clear narration from the early companions; the later scholars resolved it.

And as for the discussion of all paternal uncles and aunts and all maternal uncles and aunts, it must be known that paternal aunts are of three types: paternal aunt through father and mother, paternal aunt through father, and paternal aunt through mother. The ruling regarding them is that if there is a paternal aunt through father and mother and a paternal aunt through mother, the wealth is for the paternal aunt through father and mother. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: if he leaves a paternal uncle and a paternal aunt, if both are through father and mother or a paternal aunt and a paternal uncle through father, the wealth is for the paternal uncle because he is an ‘asaba, and there is no inheritance for any of the dhawi al-arham in the presence of an ‘asaba. Similarly, if the paternal uncle is through father and the paternal aunt is through father and mother or through father or through mother, the entire wealth is for the paternal uncle. If all are through mother, the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females. If he leaves a paternal aunt through father and a paternal aunt through mother, the entire wealth is for the paternal aunt through father. If he leaves a paternal uncle through father and a paternal aunt through father, the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle through father and a son of a paternal aunt through father, the wealth is between them with the male having the share of two females. Similarly, if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle through mother and a daughter of a paternal uncle through father, Abu Yusuf said: the wealth is divided between them by considering the individuals, with the male having the share of two females. And in al-Dhakhira: if the relation from the father and the mother come together, it is divided between them in thirds. Actually, children of ‘asaba inherit with children of fixed-share holders. If they are equal in closeness, then whoever is through father and mother is better than whoever is through father, and whoever is through father is better than whoever is through mother, and whoever is connected to the deceased through the father's relation is better than whoever is connected through the mother's relation. Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive treatment of inheritance for aunts, uncles, and their children (dhawi al-arham) according to the Hanafi school. It first categorizes paternal aunts into three types based on their relation to the deceased: through both parents, through father only, through mother only. The general rule is that the full-blood relative takes priority. For example, if a full paternal aunt and a half paternal aunt through mother exist, the full aunt gets everything. The passage then cites Sharh al-Tahawi to clarify the role of 'asaba: a paternal uncle (being a male agnate) excludes all aunts and other dhawi al-arham. Then it discusses mixed gatherings of aunts and uncles from different sides, applying the principle that when both paternal and maternal relatives are present, the paternal side receives two-thirds and the maternal side one-third. This is further subdivided recursively for those who have mixed parentage themselves. The passage also addresses the special case of 'dual relation' (dhul qarabatayn) where a relative is connected through both father and mother lines to the two groups; such a person does not exclude a single-relation relative from the other group according to the majority, but Abu Yusuf in a narration from Ibn Sama'a holds that the dual-relation relative excludes the single-relation one. Finally, it deals with children of these aunts/uncles and then extends the same principles to the aunts/uncles of the father and mother, citing al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani for the correct position.

Key Terms
بطن (batn): Level of relationship; 'different batn' means different degree of closeness (e.g., child vs grandchild). Here, when considering children of aunts/uncles, if they are at different generational levels (e.g., daughter of a daughter of a paternal aunt vs son of a daughter of a paternal aunt), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on how to divide.
أولاد الأعمام والعمات والأخوال والخالات (awlad al-a'mam wa al-'ammat wa al-akhwal wa al-khalat): Children of paternal and maternal aunts and uncles; they inherit as dhawi al-arham with the same principles of proximity and two-thirds/one-third split.

Children of aunts and uncles inherit with priority based on closeness and full-blood, and the same two-thirds/one-third rule extends to the aunts and uncles of the deceased's parents as settled by later Hanafi authorities.

Faraid — Inheritance
beginner

Children of Residuaries Take Priority Over Children of Uterine Relatives
States general rule and an example for inheritance priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for inheritance priority when children of uterine relatives coexist with children of residuaries, and apply it to a simple case.

Core Idea
Children of residuaries always inherit before children of uterine relatives when degrees are equal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays down a foundational rule of Hanafi inheritance law: when two classes of distant relatives compete — children of uterine relatives (dhawi al-arham) and children of residuaries (asabah) — the latter take priority if their degrees of relation are equal. The author illustrates with a example that may seem counterintuitive at first glance, as the daughter of a paternal uncle inherits over the son of the same uncle, a scenario that normally would give precedence to the son as a residuary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Recognize the two categories: 'children of uterine relatives' (dhawi al-arham) and 'children of residuaries' (asabah). Note that these are not the uterine relatives themselves, but their offspring.
The passage contrasts 'awlad dhawi al-arham' with 'awlad al-asabah'.
2
Check the degrees of relationship
Determine whether the degrees of the competing heirs are different or equal. If different, the closer in degree inherits. If equal, apply the specific rule.
Degree here refers to generational distance from the deceased.
3
Apply the priority rule for equal degrees
When degrees are equal, children of residuaries take precedence over children of uterine relatives. The latter are completely excluded.
This is a clear hierarchical rule: asabah offspring block dhawi al-arham offspring.
4
Analyze the example
Work through the given example: a man leaves a paternal uncle's son and paternal uncle's daughter. According to the rule, the daughter inherits all because she qualifies as a child of a residuary, while the son is considered a child of a uterine relative. Note the potential textual nuance regarding the classification of the son.
Normally, a paternal uncle's son is a residuary. The author's classification here may be due to specific family connections or a copyist ambiguity. The passage's intent is to illustrate the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضهم أولاد ذوي الأرحام فإنه ينظر إن كانت درجتهم مختلفة فالأقرب منهم أولى بالميراث وإن كانت درجتهم مستوية فأولاد ذوي الأرحام لا يرثون مع أولاد العصبة كأولاد أصحاب الفروض فأولاد العصبة يرثون مع أولاد أصحاب الفرائض بيانه رجل مات وترك ابن عمه وابنة عم فالمال كله لابنة العم لأنها من أولاد العصبة والأخرى من أولاد ذوي الأرحام
And some of them are children of uterine relatives. It is considered: if their degree differs, then the closest among them takes precedence in inheritance. If their degree is equal, then children of uterine relatives do not inherit with children of residuaries, like children of fixed-share holders. So children of residuaries inherit with children of fixed-share holders. Its clarification: A man dies and leaves behind his paternal uncle's son and paternal uncle's daughter. Then all the wealth goes to the paternal uncle's daughter because she is from the children of residuaries, and the other is from the children of uterine relatives.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage gives a general rule for inheritance when both children of uterine relatives (dhawi al-arham) and children of residuaries (asabah) are present. The rule is that when their degrees of relationship to the deceased differ, the closer is preferred. When degrees are equal, children of dhawi al-arham are completely excluded by children of asabah. The author clarifies with an example: a man leaves a paternal uncle's son (ibn 'amm) and a paternal uncle's daughter (bint 'amm). According to the Hanafi school, the paternal uncle's daughter inherits because she is considered from the children of residuaries (i.e., the offspring of a residuary), while the son is from children of dhawi al-arham. Note: In standard Hanafi law, the paternal uncle's son is actually a residuary himself, so this example may be specific to a context where the son is a dhawi arham due to other relations. The passage highlights the principle that children of residuaries (even females) take priority over children of uterine relatives.

Key Terms
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives who are not heirs through fixed shares or residuary, but inherit through uterine connections (e.g., maternal uncles, daughters' children).
عصبة (asabah): Residuary heirs who take the remainder after fixed shares, agnatic relatives.
درجة (darajah): The degree of relationship to the deceased, measured by generational distance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the degrees of children of dhawi al-arham and children of asabah are equal, what happens?
A) They share equally.
B) Children of dhawi al-arham are excluded entirely.
C) Children of asabah are excluded entirely.
D) The closer degree prevails.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, children of residuaries (asabah) always take priority over children of uterine relatives (dhawi al-arham) when their relationship degrees are equal.

Priority of Children of Fixed-Share Holders in Inheritance
Faraid example of priority among dhawi arham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which uterine relative inherits by virtue of being the child of a fixed-share holder and apply the priority rule among dhawi arham.

Core Idea
Among uterine relatives, children of fixed-share holders inherit to the exclusion of others.

This short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a concrete faraid example to illustrate a key principle of Hanafi inheritance law: when no Quranic heirs or residuaries survive, the estate passes to distant relatives (dhawi arham). Among these, the child of a fixed-share holder (walad sahib fard) is given precedence over other uterine relatives. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correct distribution in complex family structures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the survivors
List the two surviving heirs: a daughter's daughter (bint ibnat) and a daughter's son's daughter (ibnat ibnat ibn). Both are dhawi arham (uterine relatives).
2
Check for fixed-share heirs or residuaries
Confirm that there are no Quranic fixed-share heirs (e.g., spouse, daughter, mother) and no residuaries (asabat). The estate thus goes entirely to dhawi arham.
3
Apply the priority rule among dhawi arham
The Hanafi rule states: among dhawi arham, those who are children of a fixed-share holder (walad sahib fard) are preferred over those who are not. Here, the daughter's son's daughter is the child of a fixed-share holder (her father, the daughter's son, is considered a fixed-share holder by virtue of his mother being a fixed-share heir).
The daughter's daughter is also a child of a fixed-share holder, but the ruling gives precedence to the one whose connecting parent is a male fixed-share holder (the daughter's son). This nuance is upheld in the Hanafi madhhab.
4
Determine the heir who inherits
Because the daughter's son's daughter is the child of a fixed-share holder, she takes the entire estate, excluding the daughter's daughter.
The text explicitly states: 'all the wealth is for the daughter's son's daughter because she is the child of a fixed-share holder.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ترك بنت ابنة وابنة ابنة ابن فالمال كله لابنة ابنة الابن لأنها ولد صاحب فرض
And if he leaves behind a daughter's daughter and a daughter's son's daughter, then all the wealth is for the daughter's son's daughter, because she is the child of a fixed-share holder.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage gives a brief example to illustrate that children of fixed-share holders (as opposed to children of other uterine relatives) are given priority. The scenario: a deceased leaves behind a daughter's daughter (first cousin once removed maternally) and a daughter's son's daughter. The latter is the child of a fixed-share holder (the daughter's son is a residuary? Actually, in Hanafi law, a daughter's son is a dhawi arham, but perhaps the text considers him as a fixed-share holder via his mother? The ruling is that the child of a fixed-share holder (the one connected through a fixed-share heir) inherits to the exclusion of other uterine relatives. This reinforces the principle that lineage through a fixed-share heir takes precedence.

Key Terms
صاحب فرض (sahib fard): A fixed-share heir, someone entitled to a prescribed portion of the estate (e.g., daughter, spouse, mother).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is older in age.
B) She is the child of a fixed-share holder.
C) She is a residuary.

Key Takeaway
The child of a fixed-share holder (walad sahib fard) is given preference over other uterine relatives in inheritance.

Categories of Maternal Uncles and Aunts in Inheritance
Categorization and inheritance rules for maternal uncles/aunts with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of maternal uncles/aunts, apply the precedence rule, and calculate shares when paternal and maternal aunts coexist, including the dual-kinship dispute.

Core Idea
Maternal uncles/aunts are ranked: both parents > father only > mother only; the first category excludes the rest; paternal aunts take two-thirds when with maternal aunts.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out the Hanafi categorization of maternal uncles and aunts (al-akhwal wa al-khalat) based on the lineage through which they are related to the deceased. Understanding which category takes precedence is essential for correct inheritance distribution, especially when paternal aunts (al-'ammat) are also present. An intra-Hanafi dispute about dual-kinship relatives adds nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the categories: maternal uncle/aunt from (1) both parents, (2) father only, (3) mother only. Note the precedence order.
The first category has the strongest claim.
2
Apply the precedence rule
When several categories are present, only the highest-ranking category inherits. For example, if there is an uncle and aunt from both parents along with others from only father or only mother, only the first group gets shares.
Share distribution within the same category: male gets twice the female.
3
Combine maternal and paternal aunts
When a paternal aunt (from any category) and a maternal aunt are present, the paternal aunt takes two-thirds and the maternal aunt one-third. This reflects the Hanafi preference for paternal relatives.
The same applies if multiple categories of paternal aunts exist: two-thirds to the paternal side as a whole.
4
Examine the dual-kinship dispute
A relative with two connections (e.g., a paternal aunt who is also a maternal aunt through another line) does not exclude a single-kinship relative in the mainstream view (zahir al-riwaya). However, Abu Yusuf (via Ibn Sam'ah) held that the dual-kinship relative takes all.
This dispute affects the share of the maternal aunt: she gets one-third in the mainstream view, nothing in Abu Yusuf's narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأخوال والخالات فهم أيضاً أصناف ثلاثة : خال وخالة لأب وأم. وخال وخالة لأب، وخال وخالة لأم . فالحكم فيهم أن الصنف الأول مقدم على الصنف الثاني والصنف الثاني مقدم على الصنف الثالث حتى إنه إذا ترك خالاً وخالة لأب وأم وخالاً وخالة لأب وخالة وخالاً وخالة لأم فالمال بين الخال والخالة لأب وأم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين ولا شيء للخال والخالة لأب ولا للخال والخالة لأم، ولو ترك خالاً وخالة لأم فالمال بينهما أثلاثاً، وإن اجتمعت العمة الخالة أو مع مع الخال فالثلثان للعمة والثلث للخالة، وإن اجتمع عمة لأب وخالته وعمة لأم فالثلثان لقرابة الأب والثلث لقرابة الأم، ثم ما أصاب فريق الأب يقسم على قرابته من قبل أبيه وبين قرابته من قبل أمه أثلاثاً ثلثاه للقرابة من قبل أبيه وثلثه لقرابته من قبل أمه، وما أصاب قرابة أمه يقسم بين قرابته من قبل أبيه وثلثه لقرابته من قبل أمه أيضاً أثلاثاً ثلثاه لقرابته من قبل أبيه وثلثه لقرابته من قبل أمه وذو القرابتين من إحدى الطائفتين لا يحجب ذا القرابة الواحدة من الطائفة الأخرى إلا رواية عن أبي يوسف رواية ابن سماعة بيانه فيما إذا ترك عمة لأب وأم وخالة لأب وأم فالثلثان للعمة والثلث للخالة في ظاهر رواية أصحابنا . وعن أبي يوسف أن المال كله للعمة ولا شيء للخالة في ظاهر رواية أصحابنا
And as for maternal uncles and aunts, they are also three categories: maternal uncle and aunt from father and mother; maternal uncle and aunt from father; maternal uncle and aunt from mother. The ruling for them is that the first category takes precedence over the second, and the second over the third. So if he leaves a maternal uncle and a maternal aunt from father and mother, and a maternal uncle and a maternal aunt from father, and a maternal aunt and a maternal uncle and a maternal aunt from mother, then the wealth is divided between the maternal uncle and aunt from father and mother, with the male taking twice the share of the female, and nothing for the others. If he leaves a maternal uncle and aunt from mother, then the wealth between them is divided into thirds. And if a paternal aunt and a maternal aunt or a maternal uncle come together, then two-thirds go to the paternal aunt and one-third to the maternal aunt. If a paternal aunt from father and his maternal aunt and a paternal aunt from mother come together, then two-thirds go to the paternal relatives and one-third to the maternal relatives. Then what falls to the paternal group is divided among his relatives from the father's side and his relatives from the mother's side in thirds: two-thirds for the relatives from his father and one-third for the relatives from his mother. And what falls to the maternal group is also divided in thirds: two-thirds for the relatives from his father and one-third for the relatives from his mother. And a person with two relations from one of the two groups does not exclude a person with one relation from the other group, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf from Ibn Sam'ah. Its clarification: If he leaves a paternal aunt from father and mother, and a maternal aunt from father and mother, then two-thirds go to the paternal aunt and one-third to the maternal aunt in the apparent narration of our companions. And from Abu Yusuf that the entire wealth goes to the paternal aunt and nothing to the maternal aunt in the apparent narration of our companions.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage provides detailed categorization and inheritance rules for maternal uncles and aunts (al-akhwal wa al-khalat). The author explains three categories based on whether they are related through both parents, only father, or only mother. The first category (from father and mother) takes precedence. When multiple categories are present, only the first category inherits, with the male taking twice the female share. Then, when paternal aunts (al-'ammat) and maternal aunts combine, the paternal aunt gets two-thirds and maternal aunt one-third. This distribution reflects the Hanafi principle that paternal relatives take precedence over maternal ones. The passage also addresses a scenario where a relative has dual kinship (e.g., a paternal aunt who is also a maternal aunt). According to the mainstream view (zahir al-riwaya), such a person does not exclude another relative with only one kinship; the shares are distributed as per the two-thirds/one-third rule. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf (via Ibn Sam'ah) holds that the dual-kinship relative (the paternal aunt with both connections) takes everything, excluding the maternal aunt entirely. This intra-Hanafi conflict is noted but not resolved here.

Key Terms
ذو القرابتين (dhu al-qarabatayn): A relative who has two connections to the deceased (e.g., paternal aunt from both father and mother).
العمة (al-'ammah): Paternal aunt; sister of the father.
الخال والخالة (al-khal wa al-khalah): Maternal uncle and maternal aunt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All to the paternal aunt.
B) Two-thirds paternal aunt, one-third maternal aunt.
C) Equal shares.
D) All to the maternal aunt.

Key Takeaway
Maternal uncles/aunts are ranked by lineage, and when combined with paternal aunts, the paternal side gets two-thirds and the maternal side one-third, with a dispute about dual-kinship relatives not causing total exclusion in the mainstream view.

Children of Relatives: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad on Generation-Level Distribution
Sets rules for children of relatives and presents intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's methods of distributing inheritance among children of relatives with different generation levels and apply the later Hanafi resolution using analogy to the deceased's own relatives.

Core Idea
When generations differ, Abu Yusuf counts individuals; Muhammad considers the first differing lineage.

In faraid, when children of multiple generations of relatives inherit, the school must decide how to allocate shares. This passage presents a critical dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether to count each heir as an individual unit or to consider the first generation at which the lineage differs. The later Hanafi masters resolved this by analogizing to the case of the deceased's own aunts and uncles, applying the two-thirds/one-third split for paternal and maternal sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proximity rule
Understand that the closest relative takes precedence. If equal in proximity, full lineage (father and mother) over half, etc.
This is the baseline before the dispute.
2
Pinpoint the dispute trigger
When 'batn' (generation level) differs, two methods are proposed by the two imams.
The dispute is about the basis of distribution.
3
Detail Abu Yusuf's method
Abu Yusuf considers the individuals themselves (al-abdan). Each heir is counted as a unit, and the distribution follows standard gender ratios (male twice female) regardless of lineage depth.
Example: son and daughter of a paternal aunt's daughter get 2:1.
4
Detail Muhammad's method
Muhammad considers the first belly that differs (awwal baṭin). The distribution is based on the ancestral lines at the first generation where they diverge.
This can lead to different shares than Abu Yusuf's method.
5
Apply the later Hanafi resolution
For relatives of the parents (e.g., aunts/uncles of father and mother), later authorities adopted the same two-thirds/one-third split as for the deceased's own paternal and maternal relatives, by analogy.
This settles the dispute for the broader category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أولاد هؤلاء فأقربهم إلى الميت أولى، وإن استووا في القرب فمن كان لأب وأم أولى ممن كان لأب، ومن كان لأب. ممن كان لأم، ومن كان يدلي إلى الميت بقرابة الأب فهو أولى ممن يدلي بقرابة الأم. وإن اختلف بطن فعند أبي يوسف يعتبر الأبدان وعند محمد يعتبر أول بطن اختلف ويقسم المال عليه نحو ما ذكرنا حتى إذا ترك بنت بنت عمة لأب وأم وابن بنت عمة لأب وأم فعلى قول أبي يوسف المال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين هذا بلا خلاف لأن الأصول قد اتفقت. وإن ترك بنت عمة لأب وأم وبنت خالة لأب وأم وبنت خالة لأب وبنت خالة لأم فلبنت العم الثلثان ولبنت الخالة الثلث والكلام في أعمام الأب لأم وعماته وأخواله وخالاته وأعمام الأم كلها وعماتها وأخوالها وخالاتها فالحكم فيهم ما ذكرنا أنه عند الانفراد أنه يستحق جميع المال وإذا اجتمعوا من جانب الأب أو من جانب الأم أو من الجانبين جميعاً فلا رواية عن أصحابنا المتقدمين واختلف المشايخ فيه والصحيح ما روي عن الحسن بن زياد وأبي سليمان الجرجاني أن الحكم فيهم كالحكم في أعمام الميت وأخواله وخالاته حتى إنه إذا اجتمع الصنفان يجعل الثلثان لقرابة الأب والثلث لقرابة الأم، ثم ما أصاب قرابة الأب يقسم بينهم على ما يقسم بينهم لو انفردوا
And as for the children of these, the closest to the deceased takes precedence. If they are equal in proximity, then one from father and mother takes precedence over one from father, and one from father takes precedence over one from mother. And one who traces to the deceased through paternal kinship takes precedence over one who traces through maternal kinship. And if the bellies differ, then according to Abu Yusuf, the bodies are considered; according to Muhammad, the first belly that differs is considered, and the wealth is divided accordingly as we mentioned. So if he leaves behind a daughter of the daughter of a paternal aunt from father and mother, and a son of the daughter of a paternal aunt from father and mother, then according to Abu Yusuf's opinion, the wealth is between them with the male taking twice the share of the female. This is without dispute because the roots agree. And if he leaves a daughter of a paternal aunt from father and mother, and a daughter of a maternal aunt from father and mother, and a daughter of a maternal aunt from father, and a daughter of a maternal aunt from mother, then for the daughter of the paternal aunt two-thirds, and for the daughter of the maternal aunt one-third. And the discussion about the paternal uncles of the father from the mother, and his paternal aunts, and his maternal uncles and aunts, and all the paternal uncles of the mother, and her paternal aunts, and her maternal uncles and aunts, so the ruling for them is as we mentioned that when alone, he deserves all the wealth. And when they gather from the father's side or the mother's side or from both sides, there is no narration from our earlier companions, and the shaykhs differed. And the correct is what is narrated from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani that the ruling for them is like the ruling for the deceased's paternal uncles and maternal uncles and aunts, so that when the two types gather, two-thirds are given to the paternal relatives and one-third to the maternal relatives. Then what falls to the paternal relatives is divided among them as it would be divided if they were alone.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage extends the inheritance rules to children of these relatives (maternal uncles, aunts, etc.). The general principles are: closer in degree takes precedence; if equal degree, then full lineage (both parents) over half (only father), etc. A key point is the divergence between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad when there is a difference in 'bātın' (belly/generation level). Abu Yusuf considers the individuals as bodies, so if a non-relative from a lower generation inherits, they take shares based on equal division regardless of lineage? Actually, the example: leaving a daughter of the daughter of a paternal aunt (two generations down) and a son of the daughter of the same paternal aunt (also two generations down) – Abu Yusuf gives the son twice the daughter's share because they are bodies of equal generation. Muhammad, however, considers the first belly that differs (i.e., the intermediate generation), which may affect distribution. The text then discusses a more complex case with multiple types of female relatives. Then it addresses relatives of the parents (e.g., aunts and uncles of the father and mother). The earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun) left no explicit ruling for when both sides gather, but later shaykhs (mutakhkhirun) resolved it based on reports from Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Sulayman al-Jurjani, adopting the same two-thirds/one-third distribution seen for the deceased's own relatives. This demonstrates how later Hanafi authorities supplemented missing rulings using analogy (qiyas) and transmitted opinions.

Key Terms
البطن (al-batn): Generation/lineage level in the inheritance chain; a 'belly' refers to a degree of remove from the deceased.
الأبدان (al-abdan): The individuals themselves, regardless of their lineage connections.
أول بطن اختلف (awwal baṭin ikhtalafa): The first generation at which the lineages of the heirs diverge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Equally regardless of gender.
B) Male takes twice the female share.
C) It depends on the first differing belly.
D) All goes to the nearest child.

Key Takeaway
When generations differ among children of relatives, Abu Yusuf counts individuals while Muhammad focuses on the first differing lineage; later authorities resolved the broader case of relatives of parents by analogizing to the two-thirds/one-third rule.

Dual Kinship in Faraid: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on faraid calculation for dual kinship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate inheritance shares in a dual kinship scenario using both Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's methods, and identify the methodological difference.

Core Idea
Dual kinship inheritance: Abu Yusuf divides by bodies, Muhammad by ancestral lines first, yielding different shares.

This lesson dives into a rare but instructive faraid case where a single heir inherits through two distinct kinship lines simultaneously. The two great Hanafi imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on how to prioritize lineage vs. individuality. Working through their calculations will sharpen your ability to analyze complex family trees and understand the logic behind Hanafi divisions.

Guided Path
1
Map the family tree
Draw the deceased, his two daughters, their children, and the grandchild with dual kinship. Identify the two heirs: one with dual ties (X) and one with a single tie (Y).
The text describes a man with two daughters; one has a daughter, the other a son. The son marries the daughter and they have a daughter (X). Another granddaughter (Y) exists from a separate line.
2
Apply Abu Yusuf's method
Ignore lineages and count individuals as bodies. If both are female, divide equally. If one male and one female, male takes double.
Abu Yusuf's approach is simple: al-qismatu 'ala al-abdan.
3
Apply Muhammad's method
First assign shares based on the gender of each heir's parent (father/mother). X gets 2 shares from male parent side and 1 from female, total 3. Y gets 1 share. Then contested shares are combined and redivided by bodies.
This requires adjusting the base and handling fractions.
4
Compare results
Calculate final shares under each method. Note that the difference stems from whether lineage or body count is prioritized.
For the example with both female, Abu Yusuf gives 1/2 each; Muhammad gives 8/12 to X and 4/12 to Y.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان ميراث من له قرابتان من أولاد البنات اعلم أنه إذا اجتمع في الواحد أولاد البنات قرابتان وصورة هذا أن يكون لرجل ابنتان لإحدى ابنتيه ابنة وللأخرى ابن من فتزوج الابن الابنة بنت الابنة فحدث بينهما ابنة ثم مات الرجل الذي له ابنتان وترك هذه فهذه ابنة ابنة الرجل وهي أيضاً ابنة ابن الرجل وكان لها قرابتان وله ابنة ابنة بنت أخرى لها قرابة واحدة، وذكر شيخ الإسلام في شرحه أن على قول أبي يوسف القسمة على الأبدان لا على الآباء وبدنهما متفق فيكون المال بينهما نصفين وعند محمد يقسم على الآباء ويورث من جهتين باعتبار الآباء فيقال بأن التي لها قرابة واحدة لها سهم لأن أباها أنثى والتي لها قرابتان سهم من رجل لأن أباها أنثى وسهمان لأن أباها ذكر فصار المال بينهما على أربعة : سهمان يسلم لها بلا منازعة وهو مات وصل إليها من جهة أبيها الذكر تنفرد به، والسهم الذي وصل إليها من جهة أبيها الأنثى يضم إلى والأخرى أنثى وقد استويا في الدرجة فيكون المال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين على ثلاثة وأما عند محمد يقسم المال على الأباء ثم على الأبدان فيقال للذي له قرابة واحدة وهي بنت ابنته سهم لأن أباه أنثى وللذي له قرابتان فهي ثلاثة أسهم يسلم لها سهمان بلا منازعة وهو ما وصل إليها من جهة أبيها الذكر وما وصل إليها من جهة أبيها الأنثى وذلك سهم لا يسلم لها بل يضم إلى ما في يد الذي لها قرابة واحدة وهو سهم فيقسم بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين على ثلاثة لاتفاق قرابتهما في هذين السهمين واختلاف أبدانهما وقسمة سهمين على ثلاثة لا يستقيم ولا موافقة بينهما في شيء فاضرب أصل الفريضة وذلك أربعة في ثلاثة فصار اثني عشرة سهماً هذا المال جميع ومنه تخرج المسألة فإن التي لها قرابتان كان لها سهمان بلا منازعة ضربناهما في ثلاثة فصار له ستة، والذي لم يكن يستقيم بينهما مع المنازعة سهمان ضربناهما في ثلاثة فصار ستة بينها للذكر
